O children, since I have fulfilled this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, and by its completion its end has been reached, and by its chapters and its explanations it seems to me that I should supplement it with this treatise, which deals with manual operations, i.e. surgery.
For the art of manual operation is prostrate in our region and time, almost entirely neglected, so that its science has perished, and its traces are hidden; and nothing remains of it but a few brief descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been altered by their owners, leading to errors and misinterpretations such that the meanings of these descriptions are obscured and the force and art of this manual operation are prolonged.
And it seems to me that I should revive this with the arrangement of this treatise according to the method of exposition, explanation, and abbreviation; and let it come with the forms of cautery tools and other instruments of the work, as well as their addition in the explanation, and their preparation, which is necessary for this cause.
But the cause why there is not found a good artisan of manual operation in this our time is that the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy, which Galen understood to mean knowing the joints of the members and their shapes, as well as their conjunctions and separations; and having knowledge of the bones, nerves, and tendons - their numbers and arrangements; and of the pulsating veins and quiet ones, and the places where they emit.
And thus says Hippocrates with his own name that it is not a little thing in work, but especially in the art of manual operation.
But we have already said in the introduction of this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said regarding anatomy will certainly fall into error, which has caused many deaths among men, as I have seen many times; for some are formed in this science and yet depart from it without knowledge or experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced doctor incision an escrophulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and cut some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed out, causing the woman to die suddenly.
And I saw another doctor who took it upon himself to remove a stone from a man who had reached that state, and the stone was large, but he came and removed that stone with a piece of bladder, so that the man died on the third day.
And I had already predicted that he would die, and saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of his illness.
And I saw another doctor who was paid twelve ducats from our land to perform surgery, and he undertook the case of a blacksmith who had fractured his thighbone; but because of his ignorance, the doctor prepared the fracture with splints and wedges with great force, without leaving room for expansion.
And he set the bone according to his desires, and set it in traction, and left it like that for some days, commanding that they loosen the bandages; but it was done in such a way that the thighbone became inflamed with pus, leading to its ruin.
And I was called to him and prepared to loosen the bandages, but there was no calm or relief from his pains.
But the corruption was already established in the member, so that I could not stop it, and the corruption continued to spread throughout the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another doctor who pierced a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated within a few days, causing the evil to spread throughout the man's body.
This is because the cancerous ulcer is full of black bile, so it should not be treated with fire alone, but rather with something that can counterbalance it in the affected member.
And therefore, my children, it is necessary for you that manual operation be divided into two parts: one for operations that are associated with health, and another for those where danger lurks in the most severe dispositions.
And I have already mentioned this throughout the entire book wherever an operation involving error and fear is discussed, so that you should guard against it and leave it alone, so that foolish men do not find an opportunity to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your arms with diligence and protection from God, and treat your illnesses with happiness and firmness.
And use the best way to achieve health and success with the grace of God; leave behind difficult illnesses that are hard to cure; show your arms against what makes you afraid, so that your faith and your name may not waver. For it is more pleasing to remain in glory, and thus it will be later on earth and in heaven for your sanctity.
For as I have said in some of my books: “Do not treat serious illnesses unless you are skilled doctors.”
And I have already divided this book into three chapters.
Here begins the division of the book, and first the first part. Chapter One is ordered here concerning the weights of the instruments, their forms and the irons for cauterization, and what is necessary in surgery.
The division of the second part. Chapter Two on dry and wet cupping, perforation, and extraction of arrows and similar instruments, all ordered with their forms.
Chapter Three on reduction and dislocation, and treatment of torticollis and similar conditions; ordered with weights and forms of instruments.
It is necessary that I speak about the qualities of those who perform cautery and its instruments, and in what complexion it is prohibited to perform cautery.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the qualities of the surgeon performing cautery and its instruments is long and complex, subtle by science, and secret. Many wise men have already spoken about it, and they disagree among themselves; but for me, a brief mention will suffice to avoid lengthiness.
And therefore I say that cautery is beneficial for all complexions universally, both with matter and without matter, except for two complexions which are dry without matter.
In the dry and hot complexion with matter, opinions are divided. Some say that cautery is beneficial for it, while others say the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for illnesses caused by heat and dryness, since fire has the qualities of heat and dryness, and it would not be appropriate to treat a dry and hot illness with something that is also hot and dry.
"And he says that which is contrary to that, that by cautery it is done rightly in all dry and hot illnesses that come into the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its humidity to the composition of fire, you find the body of man cold."
"And I say according to the speech of that one, that I have experienced this many times in practice."
"But it is not necessary that he should come upon this who has already been examined and practiced in the chapter of cautery by recent exercise, and knows the diversity of the composition of men and the condition of illnesses in their members, and their causes, accidents, and duration."
"But in other compositions there is less fear of you, and especially in hot and humid illnesses, because all doctors agree on these and are not disagreed about the judgment with cautery in those."
"And know, my son, that concerning the secret of curing by cautery with fire and its goodness, it is about cautery in burning medicine, because fire is simple, whose action only reaches the member that is cauterized, and does not harm the other member joined to it except with little damage."
"But the action of burning medicine reaches those members which are close to it, and often it brings illness to the member with difficult healing, and often it departs."
"But fire, because of its sublimity and goodness of substance, does not do this except to superfluate. And it is already known to us through experience and the length of service in study with the art about the truth of causes; and for that reason I am excused from the length of the sermon. And if it were not that my book is not read by you in this pause, to you would be hidden the secret concerning fire, and the quality of its operation on hearts and its expulsive power through which it expels illnesses, demonstrated with philosophical speech, which would sharpen your wits."
"And know, my son, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery and consider the best time to be in spring."
"And I say that cautery is convenient at all times because the harm caused by accidental time is submerged in the benefit of what is attracted by cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary remedies for pains, strong and fistulas, which cannot tolerate delay because it is feared that malice may come, which is greater than little harm entering through time."
"And also do not keep this in your minds, my son, for that is not esteemed except by the common people and foolish doctors, because it is said that there are illnesses that are cured by cautery at any time, and they pause there."
"And the cause is not as they think for this reason, because cautery is only in place of medicine that alters the complexion and dries out the humidity which are the cause of pain."
"But nevertheless cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the volatility of its value and virtue of its operation and greatness of its lord."
"And it is possible that its illness returns at some hour of time according to the complexion of the sick person and the location of his illness, and the virtue of cautery and what has been prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities therein, and what has been brought to him in its acquisition through foods and similar causes; but the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in members with few superfluities and humidities, thus like cautery for toothache and similar ones it is possible that that pain does not return in him, but this happens rarely."
"And moreover common speech says that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is true speech because there is nothing else that they understand in it, because they believe that there is no cure that profits by medicine nor by any other cause before cautery."
"And the cause is contrary to this; and the intention that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when administering the means of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not confer benefit, and therefore cautery is administered at the end of the disease, and it profits there." "And from here on I say that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the intention which the vulgar and many foolish doctors understand."
"And they said in ancient times that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance."
"And they said that the place of cautery does not make a scar, as it is not absolutely true, because I have already proved and demonstrated that this is only done in some bodies without the others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; but however when you perform cautery with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, because it becomes ignited according to the quantity that you want due to the heat of the gold, and prepares to receive friction; and if you adjust over it ignition the gold will be melted by the fire, and some thin sheets fall into the fire for the occupation of the artisan."
"And therefore cautery with iron should be done more gently and more prudently to the correctness of operation."
"And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head among the feet, so that later when asking for something about it, you can find what you want."
Chapter one.
"This cautery confers benefit to the humidity and coldness on the second accident of which these two causes are the root, such as excess of phlegm and flux calledorrhea from the head to the parts of the eyebrows and ears flowing, and excess of sleep, and toothache and headache, and universally all illnesses that come from coldness, such as paralysis, soddenness, and apoplexy, and similar ones in those illnesses."
"The form of this cautery is as follows: first command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine purging the head for three or four nights according to what you require based on the virtue of the patient, his age and his habit."
"And then command him to shave his head with a razor and then make him sit between your hands on a cushioned bench and have him hold his breath."
"And then place the blade of your palm next to the bridge of his nose between his eyebrows." "But however, when your finger has reached the middle place, mark that spot with a point and then heat the olive cautery which is of this form:"
"And then place it on the marked spot in such a way that it descends slowly while your hand becomes gradually contracted and you rotate it."
"And then raise your hand lightly and look at the spot and if you see that some of the bone is already exposed on the head like the size of a grain of barley, then raise your hand; but if it does not return to its place with that iron, take another one which has cooled down so that what I have said to you can be seen."
"And then take some salt and dissolve it on the spot and leave it there for three days."
"And then place a piece of cotton dipped in honey around the edge and leave it there until the scar from the fire has healed." "And then treat the spot with fourfold ointment until it is healed."
"And it was said that when the wound remains open, it is better to bind the arm, for it is thus better and more healing."
"And some of them said that the spine should be cauterized within the bone, and that the cautery should be held until it burns any part of the spinal cord."
"Therefore it should be scraped with this bone which is burnt, and therefore it should be treated."
"And others said that when the cautery reaches the end of it, it should press firmly onto the bone until the bone cracks, and this is the method of small cauterization." "They consider that place where the vapors of the head expire."
"And if the wound remains open for a long time, it should be treated until it is consolidated."
"More than that, I have not seen these two kinds of cauterization in any person, except in some men according to their fear; and let it be, it seems better with health when they are; for the head is weakened by the solution of continuity from such a wound as we have testified in other members, and more so when the head of the sick man is of weak nature."
"And the first kind of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and for that reason it is healthier."
"Chapter Second."
"Of the Cautery of the Head."
"When there occurs chronic and long-lasting pain in the entire head, and this is prolonged illness, and remedies are used such as lotions, poultices, compresses, plasters, bandages, cataplasms, and similar things, and especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that does not prove beneficial for any of those causes, then consider whether the head of the sick person is of strong constitution in its nature; and if it is not weak but rather robust, then cauterize with another cautery, but not too much." "And properly cauterize there on the horn of his head with a cautery until the bone is broken." "Incise the bone and expose the quantity of bone that we have mentioned, and cauterize there with a cautery at the last part of the head where it is called 'vas', which is the place where cupping glasses are applied." "And ease your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for by doing so it causes much more pain than all other cauteries of the head."
"And I tell you this cautery in its place, for it is necessary to use the cautery with which one cauterizes the horns of the head and the occiput, which is more subtle than that with which one cauterizes the middle part of the head; and this is the form of it."
"Chapter Third."
"Of Cautery for Non-Classical Migraine."
"When there occurs sudden pain in the part of the head or a prolonged pain within the ear, let it be evacuated with medicines that clear the head, and let the other treatment that I have mentioned in the sections on diseases be administered, and if it does not help at all, then cautery is to be performed in two ways: either cautery with an acrid burning medicine or with iron."
"But cauterizing with a burning medicine is to take one handful of saltwort, and grind that up finely, and separate the extremities of it on both sides." "And properly incise the location of the pain in the temple with a sharp lancet until it reaches the bone, but so that it remains concealed."
"And then strain over it with a tight bandage by means of continuous compression, and leave that for the space of three hours; and properly remove the bandage and squeeze out the saltwort, and let the wound heal for two or three days, and then compress again on the submerged spot until the place becomes hard."
"And finally cure it with an ointment until it is healed."
"And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the second book on burning medicines."
"But cautery with iron is performed in this way: first, heat a cautery of the following shape:"
"And it is called 'clavis clavorum', for the head of it is like the shape of a nail with some curvature, and in the middle part of it there is a small elevation."
"And then place it on the location of the pain and hold your hand steady—so that the cautery does not stick to what is left—and turn the hot iron gently with speed."
"And if much of it sticks to the bone in this way, then raise your hand for fear that the artery which is left should not stick, lest blood flow."
"And then immerse the spot in saltwater and place it there, and leave it for three days; and then compress it with mustard plaster, and finally cure it with an ointment until it is healed."
"And if you wish, cauterize with the point of the cutting instrument according to this cautery, and beware of incising an artery in this migraine which is properly non-ancient."
"Chapter Fourth."
"Of Cautery for Ancient Migraine."
"But when you are treating migraine with what we have said about the previous treatment and also what we have said in the sections on diseases, but that treatment does not help, and if the disease is such that it does not respond to what has been said about the first cautery with medicine or cautery with fire, then heat the cutting cautery until it becomes red-hot, since you will mark out the location of the pain with a line of its length, or else guess where it is; and then place your hand for a moment and press down hard until you incise the artery and reach the bone."
"However, it is necessary for you to guard against damaging the continuation of the mandible, which is moved during mastication, and that the cartilage should not stick and the nerves which move it should not be damaged, and that no spasm occurs."
"And if it is about the caution and care regarding the flow of blood from the artery that is opened, since there is fear in its opening, especially for one who does not know what he is doing and is not studious and skilled; and let the operation begin."
"And I will bring to mind the regulation of accidental bleeding from an artery according to its manner in its proper place in this book."
"And if you see that this disease does not respond to this cautery and that the patient's constitution is tolerable, then cauterize him in the middle of the head as we have described, and cure the wound until it is healed."
"And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on arresting bleeding with cautery using two tweezers, since this is better cautery and more effective."
"Chapter Fifth."
"Of Cautery for Earaches."
"When in the ear there is pain from friction and it is treated with laxative causes and other treatments which I have mentioned in the section on diseases, but the pain does not subside, then heat the cautery, and this is called 'punctum', of which this is the shape:"
"And then make punctures around the ignition, and let it be moved around the entire ear like a wheel is turned, or in the circuit of each one if the pain is in each." "And go along with the cautery of the radius of the ear little by little until you have felt out the spot with the tip."
"And if you make ten punctures all over the ear and it still hurts, then treat the spots until they are healed."
"Chapter Six."
"The cautery of a tumor."
A tumor that is cured by cautery is only one of the species which comes about from blood, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
"Avoid cautery of the species that occurs due to dryness or spasm of the nerve."
When you cure that species of tumor with herbs and capperdines and guarguarisms, and your treatment does not work, then you must cauterize the disease with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
"And pause your cautery where it meets its opposite, for softening occurs only in the part that appears to be healed."
"And the manner of cauterization is that you cauterize it with a cautery in the right of the tip of the ear, a little above the horn of the head, and on the temple, its length being according to the length of the aforementioned finger, and then pause your hand with cautery until about half of the substance of the cautery is consumed."
"And this is the manner of the cautery, which is specific to surgeons, and its form is flat on one side." "But nevertheless it is more subtle than that which you have seen; and it is necessary to cut away excessive roughness with a knife."
"And then cure the place with what is usually applied until it heals."
Chapter VII.
Of the cauterization of ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient and treated with what we have said, and the patient's medicine does not agree, then cauterize them with four cauteries: over all the horns of their head; and with a cautery on the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery in the throat. And the manner of cauterization is done as has been said above.
And once cauterized over the mouth of the stomach, it becomes more severe; and then it should be treated with what proceeds from you.
Chapter VIII
On cauterization for forgetfulness caused by phlegm.
Let the patient be given large jars and pills to swallow at the beginning.
"Then shave the patient's entire head and place it on the cushion described in the treatise of plasters."
"And pause that some times, for that is the manner of cautery." "Then do this according to that method which I have already described."
"And then, if he is healed well with these, otherwise do three cauteries on the last part of his head, which should be descending from the most elevated part of the head towards the lowest part of the neck." "And pause between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and then dress the cautery with that which is produced."
"But if you want to add and the illness is tolerant of it, then do one cautery in the middle place; but if you want greater addition, then cauterize those over both horns."
"Dress that while it is healing; and let the cauteries be olive-shaped according to the form given before."
"Chapter IX"
"Of Cauterization of the Parietal Bone and of Softening of the Whole Body."
"Therefore, if you proceed in moistening the head with compresses and with that which we have said; and then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize it with one cautery in the middle place of the head, and with one cautery over all the horns of the head, and with one cautery over the last part of it, and with three cauteries over the spine of the neck."
"And if it is necessary in the illness of moistening the body too much and the illnesses tolerate it and the illness is strong, then cauterize those with four cauteries over the spine of the back, and afterwards with one cautery where the muscle of the neck has been fought, and raise the hand higher." "And then dress that while it is healing, and let this be an olive-shaped cautery."
"Chapter X"
"Of Cauterization for Epilepsy."
"It is not cauterized in epilepsy unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm."
"It is necessary that you clean the patient's brain at the beginning of treatment and in another cure, as stated earlier when the patient is severely ill except for their refusal of medicine."
"And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer purgatives and masticatories to aid digestion, beforehand for several days, with care in food preparation."
"And then shave the patient's head and cauterize it with one cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, another on the back of the head, and cauterize each horn of the head separately." "And if the patient is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize them with those same cauteries mentioned earlier, having first paralyzed and softened the body over the vertebrae of the neck and back."
"And apply cautery with olive oil as previously described."
"But if the patient is a child, then pause cauterization using this method."
Chapter XI
On cauterization for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humidity and thick phlegm, then cauterize them with those same cauteries we have mentioned earlier, having first caused paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is declining excess and the body of the patient is humid, then give them to drink that which cleanses the brain according to what has been mentioned earlier in the division on this topic.
And also shave the patient's head, and then apply a plaster made from tortoise appropriately prepared around its circumference, and then place it in the middle of the head.
And let the patient keep quiet, and be held down on all sides.
And then apply a liniment, and afterwards it is appropriate to apply an ointment made from sheep's fat. And warm this over the fire with moderate heat according to the measure that the patient can tolerate when it is applied to them. And then place it in the middle of the patient's head within the circle, and let it remain until it has cooled down.
And do this with the patient once a week until they are healed, with the other regimen of lu being followed for their health; or if you wish, cauterize them with small points according to their condition, but always keep your hand on such an ordered cautery: this is a species of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
And then apply that ointment compounded in wax or grease of swans.
Chapter XII
On cauterization for water descending into the eye.
When it has been declared that water is beginning to descend into the eye according to the signs which I have mentioned earlier in the division on this topic, then begin and give the patient to drink something that cleanses their head, and keep them from all humidity.
And have them sit in a warm bath for several days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and also cauterize it with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of water descending into both eyes, or on one side if it is only one eye.
Make incisions with a cautery along all the veins and arteries that are near the neck, and let the cauteries be in that length at the level of the temples.
And be careful not to cause fluxes of blood when cauterizing, and if you see any fluxes of blood, stop them with some medicine that is available to you.
And I will come with skill in the extraction of arteries and the removal of those, and caution regarding fluxes of blood.
Sometimes it is cauterized on both sides of the two bones near the neck with two very pointed cauteries.
Chapter XIII
On cauterization for old lacrimation (excessive tearing).
When lacrimation from the eyes is excessive and continuous, through the veins and arteries that are in the manifest location of the head externally, and it has been verified that this is a superfluity of bloody humors, then cauterize it with the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the beginning of water descending into the eyes, and also with one cautery in the middle of the head and two cauteries on either side of the temples, and two cauteries on either side of the two bones. And if you need to add more, then cauterize it also on all sides of the cheekbone over the extreme end of the eyebrow with small cauteries.
Chapter XIV
On cauterization for bad odor from the nose.
When you try to treat it with what we have mentioned in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then prepare and give the patient cochlias (shellfish) to drink for three nights; and also shave his head, and cauterize him with a medium cautery and an olive cautery. And also cauterize it with a clove cautery with two cauteries on both eyebrows just above the hair.
And be careful not to cut that artery (the nasal artery).
This is the form of the clove cautery:
Chapter XV
On cauterization for relaxing eyelids.
When the eyelid is relaxed due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery using this lunar cautery, and this is its form:
"If you wish, cauterize that above both eyebrows using two cauteries placed apart, away from the temples." "And let the length of each cautery be according to the quantity of the eyebrow."
"Press not too hard with the cautery, but according to the amount that one-third of the eyelid should be burnt."
Chapter XVI
"Regarding cauterization of the eyelashes when they grow inward and strike the eye."
"When the eyelashes grow inward and touch the eye, cauterization should be performed in one of two ways: either by cautery or with medicated fire."
"If cauterization with fire is chosen, tell the patient beforehand to lengthen his eyelashes like those that are plucked out, until they become long and hard. And if they touch the corner of their eyes, then stretch the eyelids with a bandage so that they may not be harmed while the eyelashes grow."
"When they have grown long and hard, then place the patient's head on a high table, and holding a tent-like instrument over the eyelids with one hand, make an incision with a scissor-like tool near the base of the eyelashes."
"And then place cotton soaked in egg white or mucilage of pomegranate seeds on the eye."
"Then ignite the cautery of which this is its shape."
"Then cauterize the figure that you have drawn, gradually in several views until the surface of the cord appears like the leaf of myrtle."
"The sign of health of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and its hairs raised above the puncture."
"Then raise your hand and leave that for three days. Afterwards, pause over that place with the border until the scar has disappeared." "Then dress it with ointment until it is healed."
"But if the hairs return for some cause, then remove them again and if the eyelid becomes softened, do the cautery once more just as you had done initially."
"But if the hairs do not return on the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural position and covered up so that the hairs do not touch the eye."
"But during cauterization with medicine, order the patient to let their hairs grow until they are prolonged and covered up, and then make a paper or cloth shape like the leaf of myrtle."
"Then take known soap and live coals separately. Take both quickly, mix them well together with haste so that they do not cool down. Then spread this mixture onto the paper which has been made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, place it on one or both eyes. Let the eye area be covered with cotton wool soaked in egg albumen and let the patient lie still with their head on their back."
"Then place your finger on the medicine and press it gently without moving it until the patient feels the biting sensation of the medicine, for this sensation is like that of fire."
"Then when you perceive that the sensation of biting has begun, then remove the medicine and wipe it with your little finger; then when the sensation of biting has stopped, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water. Then observe if sometimes the eyelid is raised as it should be washed away by the contraction with fire or incision, for then it will be well done. And if not, apply the medicine again to the place where the medicine did not adhere, until it becomes covered up completely."
"Then place your finger on the site with its edge until the thread of adhesion is eliminated, then heal it with ointment or with another means so that it is healed."
"And it is necessary for you to guard with vigilant diligence that no residue of the medicine falls into the eye. And if the eyelid becomes softened after several days and some cause of the hairs touches the eye, then apply your medicine again to that place where the eyelid is softened, exactly as you did at the beginning. And then avoid it until it is healed."
"And this is the shape of a myrtle leaf."
"And know that men's eyes are diverse according to their size and smallness, and therefore your contraction should be made accordingly."
"And it will not be hidden from those who study this art correctly."
"Chapter XVII."
"Of the cautery of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye."
When you treat a fistula as we have mentioned in our division of diseases and your treatment does not succeed, then you must cauterize it in this manner: Command the patient to place their head on their left side, and hold their head with some servant between your hands so that it is not moved or agitated by their head.
And then place cotton soaked in oil or wool grease on the wound.
"And then ignite the cautery whose form is as follows:"
"whose shape is hollow like a reed or an eagle's feather pen at one end where the cautery is made."
"And if you wish, it should be penetrating within the other extremity." "And if you want it to come out like this:
"However, that which is cauterized is better for one's work."
"And then press the fistula if it is open and remove from it pus and moisture." "But if it is not open, then open it and squeeze out the pus."
"And then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, on it, and hold it with your hand but a little below the cautery at the opening of the nostril, so that you do not burn your hand or cause the patient discomfort. Then pause the cautery in the gristle of the nostril and cauterize that."
"And then if at the beginning of your cautery it enters the bone, that is good. But if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice if necessary, and leave it like that for three days, and then apply a cloth with dry ointment around it until it is healed."
"But if eleven days have passed and it has not healed, then apply a caustic medicine that will expose the bone and rasp it according to what is convenient in its chapter."
"And here is another method of cauterizing a fistula that was first described: enter the opening of the fistula and dig into it with a thin camphor stick that has this shape: And on that, pour in the quantity of one dram of molten lead. Chapter XXI.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When tooth pain is caused by coldness or decay, if it does not yield to medicine, then cautery in that case is done in two ways: either cautery with the border of a cow's horn, or cautery with fire.
Take the border of a cow's horn, heat it in the fire; then quickly take it out and wrap it around the end of an iron rod. Then heat that border-horn quickly, and apply it to the aching tooth without delay while it is still hot.
And repeat this several times until the power of the fire reaches the root of the teeth.
If you wish, dip wool or cotton in the border mentioned above and place it on the aching tooth, then hold over it a red-hot iron until the iron reaches the front of the concavity of the tooth.
But in any case, the cautery with fire must be done carefully and slowly, making sure that neither the coal nor the iron is too hot to touch, so as not to cause injury to the mouth of the sick person.
Then heat the cautery to its proper shape and apply it to the tooth while you hold your hand over your mouth until the cautery has cooled. Repeat this several times, either on that day or the following day, until the pain has subsided.
And it is necessary that after this cautery, the sick person should rinse his mouth well with good water and hold it for one hour, then spit it out. This is the shape of the cautery.
And cauterize according to your will, as far as it is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter XXII
Of cautery for external fistulas.
When external fistulas are filled with phlegm and humors, and do not heal despite medication, and you wish to cauterize them slightly for digestion's sake, then heat the cautery to its proper shape, which has two sharp points, so that smoke can exit from one point while the other is heated. Apply it once or twice, as needed, until it reaches the front of the fistula.
And if the fistula is small, then apply the cautery according to its size. Leave it alone for three days and then wash the area with warm water until what was burned by the fire has healed.
Then dress it with ointment, bandages, and cloths until it is healed.

Of the cauterization of fistulas that are made naturally in the skin.

When pain occurs in some limbs due to bruising or injury and persists for a long time without alleviating with medications, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.

Chapter XLVI.
Of the cautery for leprosy.
Some lepers are occasionally treated with cautery but with little benefit, and it is harmful if the species of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore when you want to cauterize that one, consider if the leprosy is as described and treat it with what has been said in the division, and do not give it, nor this, and beware of the illness so that corruption does not spread throughout the whole body and entire complexion. Then cauterize it on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second a little below it, even to the end of the hair; and both on the two horns; and one behind, on the nape or neck.
And lastly press your hand on the cauteries a little until it impresses on the bone and causes some blisters to appear, so that the expiration of thick vapors may be alleviated from it. And cauterize the breast according to what is necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the illness and appears manifestly, then it is necessary for you to cauterize that one with those cauteries which we have spoken of in the head; and with cauteries on the tip of the nose, and two cauteries on each cheekbone, and two cauteries on each shoulder blade, and six cauteries on the lumbar vertebrae, and one large one on the vertebrae near the buttocks, and one on the buttock itself, and two on the ankles, and one on each ankle, and two on the knees and one on each knee, and two on the elbows, and two on the wrist joints; and cauterize it over all the joints of the said members and feet, and on each heel of the feet, and on the hollows of the hands.
And beware of burning the nerves which are situated above the sole that you do not burn those.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the sternum, and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more closely you apply cauteries, the greater is the benefit and efficacy; and know that the illness does not feel pain from the cautery, as people do, because its body is already stunned.
And it is necessary for the cauteries to be large or small according to the members and joints as has been described in the species of instruments.
And afterwards, cure the cautery with finely powdered barley meal mixed with honey, and with the other remedy until it is healed.

If a limb is paralyzed or numb and treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, but not healed, then cauterize that paralyzed limb with a cautery according to the size of the limb or its smallness.

Chapter XLIX
On cauterizing warts that are mole-like.
When a wart is old and does not disappear with any of the ointments of medicine, then cauterize it with a preheated cautery slightly according to the quantity of the thickness of the skin so that its whiteness and color are removed, and its color is changed.
And then dress it with flour of earth and lentils with rose oil and leaves of greater plantain, and with pigeon's blood or hen's blood, giving each one a part and mixing all together, and then apply a cloth and place it there until it heals.
Chapter L
"On cauterizing a boil."
When cancer begins and you wish to burn that tumor, then cauterize it using a circular cautery around the edge of it just as it is turning.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with the last cautery in the middle of the tumor. But I have not seen this, because I fear that it might ulcerate, and I have already seen it done several times this way.
And it is more correct to cauterize around its edge with a circular cautery as we said before, or with several cauteries.
Chapter .li
"On the cauterization of warts."
When a wart is delayed in its maturation, whether due to the superfluity causing it or because of the state of illness, either from being old and decrepit with little blood or simply due to time passing, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the wart; then cauterize around its edge using small puncturing cauteries and leave that alone until it has matured.
But if you wish to burn it with a cautery, then heat the cautery of which this is the shape: and afterwards place that in the middle of the wart until it penetrates the chain.
And if the cautery is placed too deep, such that the flesh of the arm is relieved, then dress it until it heals.
De cauterizacion de carnacuras.
Cauterization of decaying flesh.
Corrodent is wandering corruption in the limb, which corrupts it just as dry wood consumes fire.
Therefore if you wish to cauterize that corrosion with fire, first ignite several small and large cautery irons according to what is suitable for the location of the corruption.
And also cauterize it completely throughout until you eradicate all the corrosion and leave no cause of it remaining.
And then let him rest for three days, and dress the cauterized locations simply with oil mixed in until all scars are eradicated and all corruption is gone.
And then treat it with fresh flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days the flesh does not grow healthy and free from corruption, then you should repeat the cauterization on what remains in the corrupted locations.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with an acute medicine, because that is half as severe as cautery. But cautery with fire is the lighter remedy to use.
And I have already discussed the treatment of it with acute medicine in the division on that topic, so take that when you begin [the treatment].
Chapter LIII.
Of Cautery of Ulcers and Similar Conditions.
Many times in the parts of the body there appears an ulcer called a "claw," which is a thick, deep wound that causes pain. It is treated with two kinds of cautery: cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
"A hot cautery is one that ignites like a bird's feather, made of iron according to the size that contains the keyhole of the whole part, and is of fine edges."
"And then pause that ignition on the keyhole, and immediately rotate your hand with the cautery in the circuit of the keyhole until the cautery reaches the depth of the keyhole." "Leave it for three days until it begins to grow skin." "And then apply that with wild mallows crushed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, because it will eradicate its roots."
"Then treat the place with a genital ointment until it is healed."
"If it is not converted [to flesh], and sometimes it happens on the surface of the body, more often on the thighs and legs, then you should administer cauteries with water, taking a spoonful of wax or iron or bird's feather and pausing it over the keyhole; then pour in the spoon some cold water, raise your hand, and rotate that with pressure from your whole hand, so that the edge of the spoon presses into the root of the keyhole, allowing the cold water to flow onto the front of the keyhole's root; wait for a little while until the pain caused by the cold water passes for about an hour, then remove it, because the key with its roots will be eradicated."
"And thus do to those until all of them in their place in the body come together."
"And then treat those with ointments that make flesh grow, because the roots of the keys have been eradicated."
"Chapter 49."
"On Cauterization for Tremor."
When trembling occurs due to coldness in the nerves or fever caused by coldness, or similar cause, then you must cauterize them with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra; and one cautery on the foot, and on the stomach.
If the trembling subsides, it will hasten the recovery from the cold illness.
Chapter 50
On cauterization of certain accidental conditions in the body
When there are boils on the body, and pustules with openings that are filled with thick, corrupted matter, then at the beginning when they appear, they should be cauterized on top of each fistulous head with a thin cautery made from myrtle wood, the end of which is heated over fire; or with a long probe of erestology; or with a lenticular cautery.
And when such boils are seen again, they should be cauterized at the beginning of their eruption in this manner; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is dissolved by doing so, and they heal from it.
But nevertheless, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 51
On cauterization of accidental flows of blood by incision with an artery knife
Many times there are accidental flows of blood that gush out through the skin which is torn open, or through an incision of an aposteme or cautery of a member; and then they seem difficult to heal.
Therefore, whenever such an occurrence happens, then you should quickly take your knife for cutting arteries and press it upon that place with your finger stretched out so as to stop the flow of blood from there beneath your finger and prevent it from gushing out.
And immediately place on the fire olive oil cauteries, both small and large ones, by number several, and blow upon them until they are hot enough to use.
And then quickly take one of those cauteries, small or large according to the place and size where the artery has broken open, and apply it upon that vein with haste once you have removed your finger, and hold it there until it becomes adherent and stops the flow of blood.
And if blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and press on the cautery, then quickly take another cautery carefully from those which are being prepared on the fire, and do not cease doing this until the flow of blood is stopped.
And be careful that you do not burn the nerves that are nearby, lest some other harm or mischief ensue.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop the flow in those cases unless by one of four ways: either with cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it cannot be extracted, because when it is extracted, the ends of the muscle are burned and the blood is stopped; or it should be tied with a thread or with linen by strong binding, or some medicines whose virtue is to stop bleeding should be applied upon it, and they should be pressed with a bandage with proper compression.
But he who administers binding properly with linen and with bandages or by position of cauteries and the like does not deserve anything but praise.
But if such a thing happens to someone and there is no physician present nor any medicine, then one should press with the tip of the finger upon the opening of that vein as we have said, and hold it tightly so that the blood is stopped from flowing out from the highest point of the skin where the artery has broken open; and once the finger is removed from the artery, cold water should be sprinkled upon it until the blood is coagulated and thickened; and afterwards one should see whether cautery or some medicine is necessary.

Chapter III.
Of the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
When ancient migraine or sharp pain occurs due to humid and cold conditions in the temples, it is treated with medicinal remedies but this does not help. Therefore, we resort to extracting blood from the temples' arteries or cauterizing those vessels as shown previously.
The method of operation and extraction is as follows: shave the hair on the temples where the artery to be operated on is located. Understand which artery appears in the temple according to its pulse, as it may not be visible in some people due to excess hair growth.
If the artery is not visible, then apply pressure to the patient's neck with some cloth and vigorously rub the area with a rough cloth or pour boiling water on it until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then, take the scalpel which has this shape: Next, carefully cut through the skin with the scalpel until you reach the artery. Then, gently lift it up and extract the blood from its upper branches until you have extracted enough blood to reveal the underlying tunica adventitia of the artery, making sure not to cut too deeply into any other tissues.
But if the artery is too small to grasp with forceps, then carefully incise a section of it according to its size and length, making sure not to cut all the way through. This will allow blood flow to be controlled and prevent excessive bleeding.
Then, using a sponge or cloth, remove as much blood as possible from around the area within three minutes.
If the artery is large, you should ligate it in two places using strong sutures or catgut ties to prevent leakage of blood and promote healing. Be sure not to tie too tightly initially, as this may cause tissue necrosis before the wound can heal.
Then, trim away any excess skin and fatty tissue between the two ligatures, and do this promptly to prevent blood loss and promote healing.
If desired, you can cauterize the exposed artery using a hot iron or caustic agent to seal off any remaining branches and prevent bleeding. This can be done instead of tying the artery if it is too small or difficult to ligate.
It is better not to use cautery if the patient has fever or is in a state of hot complexion, as cautery increases heat and moisture, which can exacerbate the condition. It is therefore preferable to avoid this method in such cases.
After the removal of any exposed arteries, it is important to apply powder or other antiseptic agents to the wound area and dress it appropriately with dry dressings. This should be done immediately to prevent infection and promote healing. Keep the wound clean and use dry dressings until it has healed.
If during the procedure you encounter bleeding from the artery that cannot be controlled with ligatures or cautery, quickly apply pressure to the wound site using gauze or other materials. You can also use your fingers or other instruments to clamp off the bleeding vessel until it is secured and the bleeding stops. If no cause of bleeding can be identified or controlled by the above methods, then apply pressure with your finger over the site until bleeding stops. Then, rinse the area thoroughly with cold water to help reduce inflammation and promote coagulation. Finally, dress the wound appropriately according to its severity.
When extracting an artery, it is important to cauterize the vein alongside it using two hot irons or caustic agents after tying off both ends with sutures. This ensures that the intervening segment of the vessel is completely sealed off, as cautery alone may not be sufficient to seal off the entire length of the vein.
Here is how cautery is typically performed:
"Let sharpness of both knives be tested." "But if the sharpness is less than that of two knives, it is better to use two knives, for if they are very sharp like two knives close together they will cut the flesh quickly without injury."
Therefore, with those [knives] there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them, and the flesh is gently severed.
"And this work is better than all other works and much easier."
"Let the space between two knives be equal to the thickness of a finger."

Chapter X
On the treatment of swelling above the eyelid.
Axcirnath is a growth that occurs in the tissues of the eyelid above, and it happens more often in children. It aggravates their eyes and affects their sight so much that they cannot tolerate the brightness of sunlight because tears flow from them.
And therefore they make them wear patches on their faces all the time and while sleeping. And there are tears flowing from the eyelids of their left eyes which have already become inflamed and swelling is imminent in the eye; and you can feel the swelling when you press that place with your fingers.
And one way of operating is for the person to hold his head upright with his chin down and place a cloth on it and over the eye. Then make a circle according to how much swelling there is all around, and place that over it and press all around with your fingers so that swelling collects in the middle of the circle. Then make an incision in the middle of that humidity with a sharp instrument along its width, but the incision should not be bigger than the one made in phlebotomy.
But when you cut through the skin completely until you reach the swelling, for in most cases it comes out through the incision like white cheese.
Then take that out with cloths which you have wrapped between your fingers and roll them between your right and left hands until it has all come out.
And be careful not to sew the incision too tightly, lest with the instrument you reach the eye and damage it.
If it does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, then insert a small wedge with ease within that processes arternach; and then extract as we have said; and then overlay cloths with water and vinegar on the area and press them there and strain those with powders.

And then dress that wound once it has healed

"Chapter XXII"
Treatment for eye strain
This condition known as eyestrain is nothing but excess heat that settles in the area around the eye, resembling water flowing downwards.
The method of treating this condition is to make the patient sit on the prepared stool with their back straight, and then take hold of their head with both hands, moving it in such a way that you see the excess heat beginning to descend from the affected area. Then, dry it off so that it does not flow downwards, and cover them with clothing, so that they feel better as if they have seen light again.
And if it does not flow downwards, know that it is water.
But if the excess heat is not properly prepared to flow downwards as we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and take a small, fine spatula. Wipe with it on the inner corner of the eye, close to the continuation of the conjunctiva onto the cornea, until the excess heat has been dispersed.
Once it has been dispersed, then distill onto the eye a few drops of water in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then treat them with other causes until they are healed.

"Chapter XXIV."
"On the treatment of flesh torn in the nose."
When flesh of diverse colors emerges from the nose, which is sometimes caused by a condition similar to a scorpion with heavy weights or polyps in the face; this illness is called polypus because it resembles that fish. And its flesh is cancerous and pale, dark-colored; and its lion-like flesh is not dark-colored.

Therefore you should then insert a probe into it if there is any thickness, and make knots at the mouth of the wound, leaving the quantity between each knot equal to the length of the finger or less. Then extract the disease by inserting one extremity of the probe into the nostril with a hook or other means available.
And after doing this, let him breathe strongly through his nostrils until the odor reaches the canals of the nostrils and let it escape over his gullet, as many people who have done this action before can see that children do at school. It is an easy thing for anyone who wishes to do it.
And also pause both extremities of the probe, one extremity which comes out over the nostril and the other which remains in the nostril. Then apply a suture of flesh around the knots that are on the probe until you know that the flesh is well attached to the knots on the probe.
Then extract the probe and pause in the nostril until bleeding has stopped completely. Then apply a plaster made of honey and salt for three days or more until the wound is healed from the flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, insert into the nostril a lead cannula for several days until it has healed completely.
But if you have to perform an operation that requires drying, use that one accordingly.

Chapter XXVI
"Of the suture of the nose and lips, and of the ear when continuity is dissolved by plague or by injury."
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, it is advisable to perform this operation on few men.

Then cure with ointment until it heals."
But the manner of suture is that you join the solution of continuity with needles as we have explained in the suture of the belly, or join it with thread as you and others do.
Chapter XXVII

Very often it happens to many men that in wounds between their lips there appear hard abscesses like fat of hares, some small and others large.
Therefore you should cut the lips and split each of those hardnesses on all sides, then make an incision in that place with tragacanth gum until the blood is stanched, and then wash it with vinegar and cure it with something astringent until it heals.

"Chapter XXX."
"On the extraction of teeth."
"Though you treat the tooth for its pain with all art and diligence in its extraction, there is no reason why the sight should suffer when it is extracted, for it is a subtle substance that is freed from constraint by its extraction."
"When the patient is about to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which is the painful tooth; for often the pain seems to come from a healthy tooth and you extract one while the real source of pain remains until the truly diseased tooth is removed. We have seen this happen many times in dental operations."
"Therefore when you are certain which is the painful tooth and know it well, then you must carefully scrape around the tooth with a probe having some medicinal virtue until the gum is dissolved from the tooth completely parted; and then gently remove it with your fingers or delicate forceps little by little until that occurs."
"And then use strong tenaculum forceps for good grasp or extraction; carefully hold the head of the patient between your thumbs so that it does not move and thus extract the tooth straightly without breaking it, nor causing greater pain to the patient."
"And then, if it is not forgotten, take one of those instruments and insert it gently from both sides with ease and effort to move the tooth as it was done at first."
"But if the tooth is broken or decayed, then you must first fill that crack with wax and strengthen it at its end with a subtle thread so that it may not be injured during your constriction upon it with forceps."
"And you must work carefully with scraping around all sides of the gum and guard your strength so that you do not break it and injure the patient, returning upon him for more pain than the first pain of his."
"And beware that you do not do what many ignorant barbers commonly do in their haste and boldness regarding extraction, without administering anything we have recommended; for often they cause great illnesses upon people which are harder to treat than the tooth itself was to extract, and sometimes leave behind roots all broken off or some of those, or even remove teeth with pieces of jawbone as we have witnessed many times."
"And then for the extraction of it, let the mouth be washed with hot water containing salt or vinegar."
"And if there should be flows of blood thereby, as often happens, then take some quantity of sage and sprinkle it upon the wound; and if that sage does not suffice, then cauterize."
"The form of the forceps and delicate tenaculum with which the tooth is held at first should have a long extremity, short handle, and thick, so that they may not be bent when you use them."
"And they should be such that their inside is of sufficient thickness that when you grasp with them the tooth is not bent or broken at its end."
"And they should be made of wrought iron, with straight extremities, and on the extremities there should be teeth which fit into each other and grasp firmly for constriction; sometimes the extremities are shaped like the bill of a bird called the ciconia, that is, hook-shaped, and have strong grasping power."

"Chapter XXXII."
"Of the care of prominent teeth above the others."
When teeth protrude unnaturally beyond their natural course and form, especially in women, and more so in venerable elderly women; therefore you should consider: if the tooth is not adjacent to another tooth, and it is impossible to straighten or align it with thread, then that tooth should be extracted. But if it is attached to another tooth, then cut that one with this instrument whose shape resembles a pig's bristle, made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp tip.
And if your incision is not deep enough that you can cut that tooth out in three days due to its hardness, and do not touch any other teeth.
And if it's an erupted tooth that needs filing down, then file it with this Indian iron file whose shape is like this:
And all of it is made of fine Indian iron; and it has many short sharp points like this file with which teeth are slowly filed little by little over several days with ease, without touching or harming any other teeth. And finally, smooth out the tooth at its end with these little scissors.
And if it's a tooth with one corner broken off that cuts the tongue, then you must grind down that edge until its sharpness is smoothed and blunted, so that the tongue does not get cut and speech is not impaired.
Chapter XXXIII
Concerning the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver foil.
When toothache occurs due to concussion and dislocation from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot swallow food, and you have treated them with medicinal astringents, but your treatment has not been effective, then those who are strangled by gold thread and not silver may be strengthened, for silver becomes green and soon purtefies within these few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the thread be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the manner of tying is as follows: take the thread, and take twice its length between two healthy teeth, and precisely when you have made two twistings of the thread between two molars, or one or more if necessary, until the thread reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And repeat the tying within the same partition where you began, and strengthen your hand with facility and skill until it is not loose.
And let your tying be such that when you tighten the thread, you do not displace the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the excess ends of the thread with forceps or scissors, and gather them up in each place with gestures, and turn them back and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth, so that they do not cause injury to the tongue. And then leave it like this for three days.
But if it becomes loose or breaks, strengthen that one or those with another thread in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the rectification of two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as seen in these tied ones:
And when it happens that a tooth or two return to their proper places, and are repositioned as we have described, and remain stable.
And this is not done by anyone except skilled and wise artisans.
And sometimes it is appropriate to use bones from cattle for this purpose, and they are made in the shape of teeth, and placed in the location where the tooth has failed, and tightened as we have said, and remain and serve for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV
Of cutting the thread which comes into the tongue and obstructs speech.
This ligament which comes from near the tongue sometimes is natural with which people are born, or it occurs accidentally due to plaque that has already become consolidated.
And the operation in this case is that someone lifts the patient's mouth open with their head held in place, and elevates their tongue, and then cuts that nerve ligament along its length until the tongue is released from its restraint.
But if there is hardness or resistance in that place, and this is due to the consolidation of plaque, then you should use forceps and cut through it along its length until you have incised the ligament and dissolved the resistance.
And be careful that there is not made a fissure in the front of the flesh, and do not cut carelessly so that blood flows excessively.
And then wash the wound of the incision with warm water or strong vinegar or hot water and oil, and then place a lint bandage near the tongue; this is the method that the patient should follow every night so that it does not become consolidated again.
But if bleeding occurs, apply powdered vitriol over the place; but if the bleeding continues, then cauterize the place with a silver caustic which is suitable for this purpose. And then treat it with other causes until it is healed.
"Chapter XXXV."
Removal of a mucocele from the tongue
Sometimes, an accumulation of fluid resembling a small tumor forms on the tongue, preventing it from functioning naturally, and causing difficulty in speaking.
And the operation is as follows: have the patient sit facing the sunlight and examine the tumor.
If you find that it is of dark or black color, hard, and the patient feels no pain, then do not touch it for it is cancerous.
But if it is white or translucent, indicating that it contains fluid, then insert the curette into it, incise the mucous membrane around it, and excise it completely. If bleeding occurs during the procedure, pause briefly to allow for coagulation, using vinegar if necessary to facilitate hemostasis.
Then return to your work until it is completely healed with a smooth surface. It should be washed with vinegar and salt water daily, and dressed with the usual cure until healed.
Chapter XXXVI
On the extraction of tumors from the ears, those found in the throat, and other kinds of tumors.
When a tumor appears in the throat, it is called a gland, which may be formed from adipose tissue. If it is of rough color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with iron for the flow of blood; rather wait until it has matured, and then you will either draw it out or it will break on its own.
If it is of white color, round, and the root of it is subtle, then that is what ought to be incised.
And in operating on it, before your operation, consider whether the aposteme has already rested and completed its growth or if it has diminished somewhat; and then have the patient lie down facing the sun, with their head towards the east, open their mouth wide, and the servant holding between their hands press the tongue of the patient away with a noble instrument of which this is the form:

And the aposteme had already grown so much that it had formed two lumps, each of which issued forth through two ducts.

When phlegm descends to the humors and is thick and long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is at the beginning thick and round, then it is called bubo.

"Chapter XL."
"In this chapter is the summary of the sermon on the treatment of apostemes and their incision."
"Apostumes are many species with many methods, according to what is remembered about them in this book, each one being described here:"
"And they are different in their puncture and incision methods due to two ways: one is for the species of deep apostemes; and by what is gained from humidity."
"And the second way is according to the places where accidents occur in the body, for the aposteme that occurs on the head is different from the one that occurs on the buttocks; and the aposteme that occurs on a fatty location is different from the one that occurs at a joint; and each of them has its own treatment through operation."
"And more about apostemes: some should not be punctured but rather waited until maturity or completion of them in those areas:"
"There are other apostemes which should be drawn out when they are hard and not mature according to their completion, such as apostemes near joints, for when an aposteme is near a joint its essence extends into what is around it, and often the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted by it, causing contraction of that limb; or if it is near a main limb, if you delay its puncture until it is fully matured it may harm the main limb; or if it is near the buttocks, then pierce it immaturely; and do not pierce one that is hard due to undigested matter unless it is near the buttocks, for fear of tearing the skin of it and extending it towards the inside of the buttocks, causing a fistula." "But you should know the time of puncturing an aposteme, which is when it has matured:"
"And it is matured when there is cessation of pain and fever, and around the decrease in redness and pulsation, and the sharpness of the head of the aposteme, and other signs."
"And because you should know that puncturing should be done where there is least pus: far from the aposteme and coming from its peak."
"And puncturing should always be done along the length of flesh or skin if apostemes are in areas such as those around the breast or feet, and at locations of muscles, nerves, arteries; and, finally, in all places except where there is reflection; for those that are reflected use puncture according to that limb."
"But when an aposteme is in a fatty location, it is better to leave its puncture until digestion of it has been confirmed according to its completion, as we have said; for if you pierce it before that time the skin of the hole will be extended and much matter will accumulate, and often the lips of the hole will become hardened and its depth increased."
"But some apostemes are punctured across the width of flesh or skin due to necessity, or according to what is necessary for that limb."
"And you should administer a small puncture, and in large apostemes make a large puncture and wide or many incisions according to the diversity of their size."
"And others are apostemes in which it is necessary to make the chain concave and cut it when it has been completed as if it were bread, and done so that it may already stick, such as those that occur in tubercle and buboes."
"And of those that are incised with three angles in section; and of those in which it is cut like the figure of a mitre leaf, such as those of hermits."
"And among them are those to which round incision and crescent incision are administered, similar to those."
"But that one to which it is not applicable as with their superficial apostemes, you should pierce it with superficial or flat puncture alone."
"And when an aposteme is large and has already collected much matter, you should pierce it but not remove all the pus at that time; having removed some of it, bandage the aposteme overnight, and then on the following day remove a larger portion, and continue doing so gradually until it has healed."
"And especially if the patient is an infant or pregnant woman or child or elderly person in decline, for the animal spirit is often resolved with the exit of matter, and sometimes the patient dies, and you operate on them."
"Therefore guard this chapter with great care, and especially the puncture you make in these apostemes, for you should cleanse the wound and consider whether the wound of the aposteme is small or is a flat section; then you should administer the cautery to it or ancient caustic. And if the aposteme is large and the incisions of the puncture are many, then you should pause between each pull until they converge together."
"And if in the aposteme a portion of the chain is cut off or has been made concave around its circumference, then you should fill it with old cotton or thread made from linen cloth, like when you bake bread or cakes without moisture; and strengthen it on the third day; and then proceed with it and cure it with what is needed for the dressings until it heals."
If blood flow occurs during your operation, then you should administer warm water and vinegar mixed in a cloth, and place that over the area of blood flow many times.
If bleeding persists, then you should administer remedies and care as recommended in various places of this book and its division.
If it is wintertime and the location of the abscess has many nerves nearby, then you should mix powder in warm vinegar and oil, and place that on those locations.
And if it is summertime and the locations are fleshy, then you should mix powder with water and oil or vinegar and oil, and keep that warm until the third day, as we have said, and then you should drain the abscess and cleanse it, administering to it whatever is appropriate for its healing.
And this is what is necessary for the general knowledge of the treatment of abscesses, but according to the method of distinction, I have already mentioned in detail how each type of abscess should be treated in its own chapter.
Chapter XL.
Of the Incision of Abscesses Which Appear on the Neck
In the neck of young children, small abscesses form, which are like glands containing sacs that are made in this way; and their varieties are many.
And among them there are pimples, and those contain a moisture similar to slime. And those which contain moisture resembling dust also resemble them in this way; and those which are like stones, hard.
"I fear not the sectioning of all things, nor the extraction thereof; for I must do that which is neither harsh nor cruel."
"You must use an instrument shaped like this, called a 'tomes', so that you know what it contains."

Chapter XLIX.
About incising abscesses that occur from injury or by venesection

Chapter 1 of the abscess which occurs from twisting of nerves
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves when they are subjected to percussion or excessive movement, and for similar reasons.
And such abscesses most often occur in the locations of the elbows or ankles, or in any place where joints are moved with violence.
And if the abscess is large, it resembles the color of the rest of the body; it is hard and tense, without its usual flexibility, and when pressed with violence, there is a sensation like stupor.
And if the abscess is not situated on the chest, it is near the vein; it is moved at any part and does not move toward its front or back.
Therefore, those that are located in the joints should not be bandaged with iron, lest it cause contraction.

But if it is large, then suspend it with a bandage, scrape it off, and remove it; then sew it up with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.

Chapter 52
Of the treatment of umbilical hernia
An umbilical hernia is caused by various factors: fissures of the skin over the belly, or a prolapse of the intestine or bowel through other ruptures; blood flowing from a vein or artery as mentioned earlier; or constricted flatulence in it.
But if it is due to a skin fissure and prolapse of the intestine, then the color of the abscess will resemble that of the body; there will be no pain, and it will appear in various positions. But if it is due to prolapse of the bowel, then its position will be as we have described earlier with great variation. When you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then return; there will be a loud noise, and it will become greatly enlarged during bathing and vigorous exercise.
But if it is due to moisture, then there will be no noise and it will not disappear or become hidden when you press it with your hand; nor will it be constricted or enlarged.
If it is due to blood, then appear these signs: the abscess turns black.
However, if it is due to flesh without blood, then it becomes a hard, large and tough abscess, which grows in size.
But if it's due to windiness, then its touch is light.
And the operation in this case is to consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to arterial blood, venous blood, or windiness; therefore you should be prohibited from treating it, because there is fear and awe as I have taught you in the chapter where I spoke about arterial and venous abscesses.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to bowel or tissue, then you should command the patient to keep their elbows by their sides and stand extended. Then, sign with a tent around the entire navel, and then command them to lean back between your thighs but above your two knees, and then encircle around the navel with a wide spatula on the area where you have indicated with the tent. Then extend the mass of the abscess upwards with a large unguent; and finally, ligature around it with strong flax or cotton, using an appropriate tying manner.
And then carefully press the middle of the extended abscess towards the cord and insert your finger into that opening, seeking the bowel. And if you have found it and grasped it with the cord, then gently pull it outwards and push it back inside the abdomen.
"And if you've exposed the tissue, then take that with oil; and incise its excesses."
"But if it's opposed to your artery or vein, then encircle that spot and return to your operation; take two needles and place them in those strong threads, inserting the needles into the circle you've made around the abscess in a cross-like manner, penetrate them; and then stretch the abscess in four directions over the needles."

"And if the prominence of the navel is made of raw flesh or due to dampness, then you must remove the abscess as we have said and drain out the blood or dampness that you find there, and then dress it with oil until it is consolidated."
Chapter LIII
Of the treatment of cancer.

And the operation for this kind of cancer is as follows: first excise the malignant tissue and purge with black collyrium several times, then cauterize it if there is a manifest tumor.
