"On treating panaricium (a whitlow) by rubbing with ointment, making an incision in the nail and adding a finger, and cutting it off."
A pimple is an abscess that appears beneath the nail of the toe or finger, and often appears in other digits as well. When it becomes prolonged and its cure is more difficult, it is called an abscess of an abscess or it is corrupted, and it emits a thick liquid that corrupts the root of the nail. Often the entire nail becomes corrupted, and frequently its corruption extends to the bone, which gives off a foul odor. The tip of the toe or finger becomes reddened, and the color appears dark.
Therefore, when you have that which we have spoken of in the division and your cure does not confirm itself, it is necessary then to cut with iron all the superfluous part that remains of the nail and cauterize the wound immediately afterwards, for cautery is very helpful in this case.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the corners of the nail begin to push into the flesh beneath, causing pain and inflammation there, then you should make a subtle incision at the angle of the nail that is pushing into the flesh, lift it up, and cut away that flesh with ease. Then place over what remains of the flesh medications that burn corrosively until all is removed; and immediately afterwards dress that wound with an ointment so that it may heal.
But if the corruption has already entered the bone, then you should cut into the bone and extract it, for there is no time when the bone can be healed once it becomes corrupted.
And if the corrosion and corruption extend to the finger itself, then you should cut into it at one of its joints according to what is remembered regarding its movement, and dress that wound until it heals.
But if the nail receives a blow or rubbing and there is violent pain in that place, then you should first bleed the affected part. Then dry the nail with a sharp spatula made transverse from above to below, taking care that the cut does not extend into the flesh beneath the nail. Otherwise you will cause severe pain and provoke the formation of excess flesh in that location. And dress that place with something that relieves pain.

"Regarding cutting for worms generated under the skin and called bovine disease."

"Regarding cutting for the disease called nafir."
"This disease is called in our land 'nafir' and it is pain that comes to some members and also changes from member to member."
"And you will see this as I will describe it to you." "I was called to see a woman patient in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the elbow joint of the arm, and after it had been broken for an hour, I saw that inflammation moving thus as if a worm were pushing towards this place but more lightly than could be seen, just like flowing liquid silver from place to place."
"And then the pain moved away from that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and stayed there for an hour."
And now correct what was done in the other body until it reaches the other arm.
And now I recall that you said to turn yourself throughout your body according to what I saw; and he was greatly amazed at the transformation of him and the speed of member by member. And I had already seen before that in my eyes according to that manner, but I have heard many who affirm that it hurts to be transformed from member to member. But I do not see that in the eyes like in women.
And you think that is because the woman was of old age, where there are many bodies and veins uncovered, and in this appearance it seems to feel that windy transformation.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this backwards curve in those who are delightful and have moist bodies with veins intertwined.
And when you want the cure of him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on him gently and let go lightly and cautiously over him where that windy oppression has come out, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then cure him with exercise of the body and with that which expels windiness, and treat him with pills made from bitter herbs and with pills made from saffron and similar things.
Of removing arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their species and according to the places of the body where they strike, because their diversity is that some have large points and some small and concave ones, some are blunt and others solid. Some have three angles, others have tongues or feathers.
And the signs of death from being struck by an arrow are: when it passes through the bone and exits from the cranium above the brain, because it happens suddenly with loss of consciousness, vertigo, roaring in the ears, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasm and confusion of understanding and vomiting of bile. The breath is suddenly cut off and speech is destroyed, and there is an exit of moisture resembling white powder from the wound, and a yellowish discharge like watery matter comes out of the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treating and extracting the arrow unless it is outside of the body.
But the signs of an arrow when it strikes the heart are: it is near the left nipple, and it seems to be stuck in hard flesh and not soft. The movement of the arrow is like the beating of the pulse, and there flows black blood from the wound, there is coldness of the extremities, chills, and syncope; therefore know that death will come without delay.
And the signs when an arrow has pierced the lung are: there is emission of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck become obstructed, the complexion changes to pale, and there is great expiration or sighing for breath, and a desire for cold air.
And if the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then the pain will be near the little ribs, and there will be great swelling of the lung with violent pain and sighing, and all the members of the muscles will become paralyzed.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then there will be immediate vomiting of some undigested food from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and is stuck there, then there will be some opening of the wound, or the skin, or a ruptured intestine, and there is no hope for cure by extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder and urine is expelled from outside, and there is some process around the parts outside, then there will be great pain above the wound, therefore know that it is mortal.
But the other members such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, arms, and shoulder blades, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and their like are saved according to the most favorable conditions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I advise you to consider this concerning these arrows, so that you may know how to proceed with their treatment.
For example: there was a man who received an arrow in the lacrimal bone of his eye near the root of his nose, and I extracted it from another part, leaving behind only the graze of the ear, and he healed without any harm to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which had struck him in the graze of his eyebrow, leaving behind only the eyelid. The arrow was already hidden and could not be seen, except for a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft. It was a large, pointed arrow of the Turks, with a lion-headed ferrule, which had no feathers.
And the Jew healed completely without any harm to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat. It was an Arabic arrow with feathers, and it was already submerged in his throat. Then I incised between two guide fingers and carefully extracted it, and the Christian man healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who had received it in his belly and thought that he would die from it. Therefore, since thirty days had passed without any change in his condition or symptoms, I incised over the arrow and removed it, and he healed without suffering any serious harm.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had struck the back and the wound had healed over it. After about seventy years, the arrow came out at the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow had struck her belly and the wound had healed over inside her without any change in her condition or symptoms. The woman did not suffer any harm to any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow struck his face and the wound healed over and the arrowhead broke off without causing much pain. Many similar cases have been seen to this effect.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the princes of the king which had struck in the middle of his nose and had deviated slightly to the right. The entire arrow was hidden.
And I was called for the treatment of him about three days after he was struck by the arrow.
And then I opened the wound of the arrow quite narrow. And then I inquired about it with a delicate touch and did not feel it; but he felt a puncture on the right ear, and therefore I believed that this puncture was due to the extremity of the arrow.
"I have plastered the area with a plaster that has attractive and digestive virtue, ensuring that the area is covered so that I can see the arrowhead, for it is dry."
"There was no accidental occurrence in the area where the arrow had reached; and I continued applying the plaster for many days without any accident occurring; and the wound has since healed, and the patient recovered from his injury within several days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and recognized it."
"Then I applied acute corrosive medicine on the wound for several days until it opened; and with the forceps I grasped that [the arrowhead], and extracted the thin extremity of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft."
"And then I applied that acute medicine on the opening of the wound until it appeared visible to me; and I spent some time with this for the period of four months."
"And then when the wound was enlarged and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, I grasped that [the arrowhead], but I did not succeed in extracting it immediately. Then, however, I did not cease subtly manipulating and devising ways with ingenuity until one day I began to remove it by using methods of extraction described below, according to what came at the end of this chapter, so that I was able to extract it; and then I restored that wound."
"And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I did restore it and the wound became consolidated, and the illness was completely healed, without any remainder or cause for complaint."
"And I recount to you the method of extracting certain arrows because I have not given any rule or significance regarding that which has not been mentioned in this narrative, for the parts of this art and the definitions thereof are not understood with a mere narration nor is the book itself comprehended."
"And the wise artist compares little about matter; and that which is present he considers more than that which is absent; and he discovers new operations and new instruments whenever foreign causes of the art occur."
"And therefore I say that arrows are not forgotten in the members into which they have been fixed, but rather according to two methods: either by attraction of the place where it enters, or by the opposite method of withdrawal."
"And that which is cast in by the entrance point, or is an arrow proceeding from a fleshy location, is drawn out and extracted."
"And if it does not obey extraction at the time when cast, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh that is around it becomes softened, and so that its attraction and exit may be alleviated."
"Similarly, if it is fixed in bone and does not obey extraction, then you should leave it for a few days and repeat the traction and motion each day, and then it will be extracted."
"And if it does not obey extraction even after several days, then you must perforate around the arrow in that bone with a subtle awl so that the location may be widened by the arrow, and then extract it."
"But if the arrow is deeply buried in the location of the body and concealed from sensation, then you must search for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then extract it with some instruments which are suitable for its attraction."
"And if you cannot reach it due to the constriction of the wound and the length of the probe, and there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein there, then cut through the flesh until it is widened so that it may be possible to extract the arrow."
"And if it is held by the ears with which it may be retained, then separate the flesh in which it is fixed on all sides with whatever effort you can make; and endeavor to achieve this without fracturing the ears or twisting them, so that you may succeed."
"And when you administer the extraction of the arrow in any location, then use twisting with your hand and forceps on all sides so that it may obey; and do this with such subtlety that the arrow is not broken, and so that its extraction may be easily accomplished."
"And if you cannot accomplish this at that time, then leave it for a few days until the surrounding parts have softened, and then repeat it since it will be alleviated." "But if you find that there is opposition to the flow of blood, then administer what we have said about cure in its chapter."
"And guard against injuring any vein or nerve or tendon, and exert all possible effort to avoid the arrow; and do this with gentleness, calmness, slowness, and steadfastness as I have recommended."
"And therefore when you extract the arrow, do so in such a way that the illness is according to the figure according to which the case of the arrow was situated in it, for this is more beneficial." "But if this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you according to the figures."
"But arrows which are extracted against the other side of another part, or those from which some part has already proceeded into other parts outside it, or those which have their point or end concealed due to sensation of the flesh above them and are prominent in that area; then cut through that area. Let the section be according to the quantity which the forceps grasp; and then extract it, for it will be easy to extract."
"But if it is retained in the bone, then twist it backwards according to its opposition until the arrow presses against the bone and widens it on both sides, and then extract it."
"And otherwise, leave it alone for a few days, and then repeat it when it is necessary to do so."
"If the arrow's shaft is in that [place], then you push it there with force."
"But if the shaft has fallen and you wish to administer momentum, then insert it there, if it's rigid, use a smooth instrument concave so that between the concavity of it and the end of the arrow it pushes gently against it."
"But if the arrow is concave, then you push it with an instrument inserted in that, because the arrow is opened by it."
"And if the arrowhead is poisoned, then you must cut away all the flesh around where it is inserted, if that is possible for you, and immediately treat it with whatever remedy you deem appropriate."
"And if the arrow strikes bone, or enters the abdomen, bladder, side, and is close to touching it with your hand, and if you can safely cut around that area, then cut around it and guard against cutting any veins or nerves, and extract it, and apply a bandage if necessary, and treat it until it heals."
"Forms of forceps with which arrows are extracted:"
"[They] have the ends like the beak of a bird, made sharp like a saw when they grasp the arrow so that it does not slip away."
"And there are many types of them: large, small and medium-sized ones according to the amount of the size of the arrow and its thickness, and also according to the width of the wound and the constriction of it."
"like a rod, in order to facilitate the entrance of the concave arrowhead and push it with that."
"On flebotomy of veins:"
"The veins that are usually flebotomized are thirty-two in number, of which sixteen are in the head. These include two pulsating veins near the ears called 'alizain'; two herbs on the temples; two veins on either side of the nasal cartilage called 'aspicients'; one vein in the middle of the forehead; one vein at the tip of the nose; two branches of the neck; two veins above the upper lip; two veins below the lower lip, which are called 'aleglic', and two veins on either side of the tongue." "In the arms there are five veins that are flebotomized. Of these, one is cephalic and is located on the outer side of the arm; it is called 'the vein of the head' by the people. Another is median and is composed of a branch from the basilica and another from the cephalic vein; this is called 'the vein of all the body' by the people. The basilica is a vein that lies on the inner side of the arm, called 'citillar' by the vulgar people. There are also the funis veins located above the elbow, in which flebotomy is usually performed and where it appears most visibly on the forearm. Lastly, there is the salvatella vein located between the little finger and the next one."
"And in the foot and ankle there are three veins: one on the outer side of the curvature of the little finger of the outer side of the foot, another is the saphenous vein located on the inner side of the ankle on the inner side of the foot, and the third is the sciatic vein located on the outer side of the ankle on the outer side of the foot. There are also other veins in the ankle that resemble these."
"The benefit of flebotomizing the veins near the ears is for treating ancient catarrhs, migraines, and headaches, as well as ulcers on the head that are of long standing."
"And the method of performing flebotomy on these veins is as follows: first, shave the head of the patient and rub it with oil on the occiput where both veins are located. Then, place a wide cloth on the head of the patient and apply firm friction. Next, wrap a bandage around the patient's neck so that both veins appear near the ears in two depressions on the head. Finally, grasp those veins with your thumb and if you feel their pulse with your thumb, then proceed to perform the flebotomy."
"Then take the cutting lancet and insert it into the vein on the arm until blood flows to the bone." "And then raise your hand with the vein and arm upwards and make an oblique incision into the vein, cutting it obliquely." "And let the length of the incision be about two fingers or thereabouts. Then allow as much blood to flow out as desired."
"And then apply powder and a bandage, leaving it in place until healed."
"And it has been preceded at the beginning of this book, the incision of those affected by cauterization."
"The two veins leading to the temples, which is beneficial for phlebotomy, are useful for ancient headaches and persistent migraines, and for frequent eye disorders. It also has sharp, abundant effusions in the hips."
"And the quality of performing phlebotomy on them is as follows, which I recommend to you: compress the patient's arm with a bandage until both veins appear clearly at the surface and are demonstrated by touching them. Then proceed."
"Then raise the arm over the vein at its intersection with your index finger, insert the phlebotomy lancet into it, and elevate the vein above. Incise it as you have done on other veins, and let the blood flow according to your need."
"And then release the compression from the patient, keep your finger on the vein for an hour. Then apply cotton and powder, and strengthen that area with firm pressure. Leave it alone until it is healed."
"And already in the beginning of this book, I have described their remembrance and incision, and attraction."
"And therefore phlebotomy of the frontal vein is appropriate for treating ancient headaches, migraines, and severe or foul ulcers on the face."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on it is as follows, which I recommend to you: have the patient compress their forehead with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called 'fossor', whose shape is as follows:"
"Press the pointed tip of the 'fossor', which is at its head, against that vein and pierce it with a quick movement or another cause. Let the blood flow according to how much you want. Then release the puncture from the patient and compress it until it is healed."
"And sometimes it is useful to perform phlebotomy in a lateral direction, but it is not necessary that the phlebotomy be of acute termination like other phlebotomies. Rather, it should be performed transversely because the bone is nearby and would easily break if the phlebotomy were too fine."
"But the advantage of these two veins that are on the lacrimals of both eyes for scabies of the eyelids due to heat and sweat in them, and for diseases of the face."
"And the quality of phlebotomy is this: have the patient compress their eyelid with a bandage, then perform phlebotomy on it while you are standing over their head."
"And the phlebotomy should be performed transversely and slightly diagonally with a fine phlebotomy needle because there is little flesh in that location. If the phlebotomy is too fine, it will easily break."
"And then make the blood flow as much as you need, and pause over those wounds. Then compress them for one night, and finally release them."
"But phlebotomy of the nasal vein is beneficial for acute fever and sudden sneezing, and for diseases of the face and migraines that occur there, especially when they are chronic."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on it is this: have the patient hold their head and then take your left hand and place it over their nose. Then take a fine, long phlebotomy needle and insert it into the middle of the head of the nose in a straight line because the vein does not appear by touch here, and blood will begin to flow soon."
"It is necessary that you slowly withdraw your hand with the phlebotomy needle and make the blood flow according to your needs. Then compress it for one night because it will heal easily."
"But the benefit of phlebotomy on both wrists is for constriction of the armpit and the beginning of leprosy, as well as for many diseases, itching, and corrupting ulcers like herpes."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on them is this: have each patient hold their arm with a cloth. Then stand over the head of the patient and have the patient sit on a seat."
"And then perform phlebotomy on the vein along the arm with a wide, fine needle little by little. Then remove blood according to a moderate quantity or as much as is needed. And do the same for the other vein."
"And then release the cloth and compress both veins with moderate pressure so that the patient is not fainted. Then leave them for the night because the wound will have healed by morning."
"And the benefit of phlebotomy on the veins in the armpits is for small apostemes and pustules of the mouth and corruption of the gums and ulcers and fissures, as well as ulcers that are in the nose and around it."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on them is this: have the patient sit in front of you and compress their head with a bandage. Then turn their lips up and look for two veins, one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left. The demonstration of each is by its darkness, and this is more so because there are small black veins around them. Then incise each with an incident incision."
"But if you have any doubt and do not know which is one of them, then listen to the sound of their pulsation or observe the visible pus of those."
"And perform the same on both veins that are in the upper lips."
"It is appropriate to perform flebotomy of the cephalic vein at the forearm, in cases of uvula inflammation, and mouth infections."
"And the quality of flebotomy is such that it should be done between the thumb and index finger, towards the sun, raising the tongue and observing both sides of the vein, noting the change in color to black."
"And then flebotomize each one; be careful not to exceed your hand in flebotomizing those, for there are arteries beneath them."
"And the veins that are flebotomized in the elbow are those in which blood is customarily drawn from all men. The flebotomy of these is performed in two ways: either by putting a mirtilated or olive-shaped lancet to it with subtle inclination; or by cutting with a cuticular flebotomy lancet, called 'alnessil', and this is the shape of it:"
"It should have edges like this: so that it may open the concave veins full of blood appearing thickly, and those which contain dark, turbid blood."
"And this flebotomy has less width and more subtle extremity; it is suitable for flebotomizing the subtle veins that contain clear, citrine-colored blood."
"But the benefit of flebotomizing the basilica vein, which is one of three veins, is that it draws out blood from illnesses that are located in the head and neck, and that which affects the feet and belly."
"And it is convenient for the flebotomist, when flebotomizing this one, that he guard this carefully and be above all fearful of it, because beneath it there is an artery. If he errs in the puncturing of the flebotomy lancet, he will incise that artery and cause a flux of blood to flow forth, therefore he should not flebotomize this with a puncturing flebotomy lancet, but rather cut with alnessil."
"And if the basilica is not apparent by manifest appearance, then you should leave it and move to another, seeking some of its branches; or you should flebotomize in place of that the end of the arm where it is manifest, and cut that with an alnessil flebotomy lancet as we have said."
"But if you wish to flebotomize the elbow, then first at the thickness of the arm where you feel the pulse, press gently upon it with your thumb; and raise the arm and cut the vein with a concave section using an alnessil flebotomy lancet as we have said; and guard against percussing near some space of the location of the artery."
"And when you bind with a bandage, if there is swelling in the place that you have pressed with your thumb, then this swelling is swelling of the artery; therefore leave it."
"And if the blood flows in the flebotomy like a child's urine and the blood is thin red, then know that it is the blood of the artery; and therefore tighten your finger above that for some space; and then remove your finger, and if the blood is cut off, because many times it is cut off, then bind the arm and leave it."
"And guard against the illness of the wound, and be above all fearful of it, and do not move that for some days until it is healed."
"But if the blood is not cut off and yet it continues to flow, then this medicine is not present; therefore you have incised the artery if it appears to you. Because the extremity of it is contracted and the blood is cut off, or take some cotton wool, compress that, and apply it over the location of the vein with pressure using a bandage and powder for another day."
"And therefore if the blood is cut off, good. But if not, then cure it with that of which we have spoken at the recollection of the location of the powders cutting off flux of blood."
"And the stopping of the flow of blood from it is difficult in many dispositions because of the smallness of the wound and the possibility of tying the arm."
"But the flebotomy of the black vein is beneficial because it draws out blood from the upper part of the head and from below the body. Because it is composed of branches of the basilica and branches of the cephalica, as we have said."
"And it is necessary for the flebotomist to be above all fearful in the flebotomy of that one because below it there is a nerve. And therefore if he errs in the puncturing of the flebotomy lancet and strikes the nerve, then numbness will occur, and its healing is difficult and sometimes it cannot be completely healed. This nerve can often be felt; but if it is hidden to some people and is subtle and does not appear, then you should pause the flebotomy with the lancet with which you are flebotomizing with a concave section, and avoid the nerve with your strength."
"And if the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein along its length."
"The benefit of phlebotomy on the temporal vein is that it draws blood from the head and alleviates illnesses of the eyes."
"And this is where you should puncture if you want to perform phlebotomy with the olive style or the myrtle style; for it is the safest vein, since neither artery nor nerve is left."
"But sometimes an abscess forms in some people if they are not phlebotomized during the first percussion; but do not touch that abscess."
"The first thing you should know is that phlebotomy is administered only for the preservation of health and in hopes of preventing illnesses, and with caution against their occurrence. And that phlebotomy should be performed on one of the veins that are located on the elbow, namely: the cephalic, the basilic, and the median cubital vein; and that it should be performed at the beginning of spring, after fasting in spring and autumn, and near mealtime in winter, when signs ofpletion appear."
"And phlebotomy should be performed on the third day or even earlier, provided that at least three hours have passed since the previous meal."
"But phlebotomy should be administered whenever it provokes convenience and necessity for that person, whether at night or during the day, at any hour and at all times."
"And children should not be phlebotomized until they have reached the age of fourteen; pregnant women should not be phlebotomized until the fourth month has passed from conception to the seventh month; and the elderly, once they have surpassed seventy years of age, should not be phlebotomized."
"And before anyone wishes to be phlebotomized, they must cleanse their intestines with a lion's clister if there is much retained feces in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities from the purification of the intestines during phlebotomy, which would harm the principal members."
"And patients with sacred illness or drunkenness should not be phlebotomized until they have recovered from their condition; nor those who are suffering from fever or intoxication. Phlebotomy should be avoided near colic pain and vomiting, and near abdominal complaints, and near multiplication of coitus and work and exercise during fasting or vigils, and due to any cause that dissolves the virtues, i.e., bodily or animal."
"And before phlebotomy, consider the refinement of humors, and if the blood is thick, use foods and drinks to thin it out, and medications if possible."
"And before phlebotomy, take a bath if there is no prohibition due to any cause, or stimulate with some exercise so that the blood may be subtilized."
"And pause phlebotomy at the foot of the day, especially around the hour of terce, as we have said."
"And perform phlebotomy only when the foot of that day is guarded from all harmful animal accidents, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and from all harmful bodily accidents, such as excessive work, fatigue, coitus, etc."
"And at his seat, let there be present the causes in use which are customary for men with aromatics and perfumes, balms, etc., according to their quantity of possibility."
"And let the phlebotomist sit on a stool that is higher than the stool of the person who is to be phlebotomized."
"And first, extract his arm and rub it with the phlebotomist's hand two or three times to warm it up; then tie the limb with a bandage twice."
"And let the tying be moderate, for if it is too tight or there is an excess of superfluity in the tying, it will impede the flow of blood; but if it is too loose, it will cause weakness of the blood."
"And after tying, let him who is to be phlebotomized rub his hands together until the veins are swollen and can be felt."
"And the phlebotomist should anoint his lancet with a little ancient olive oil specifically; and then place his left index finger on that vein where he wishes to perform phlebotomy, so that the vein may swell slightly and the percussion be steady, for one of the veins which is about to be opened will throb like a string, and that one which is full of windiness will contract under the lancet, revealing itself to the phlebotomist; and he should not open the vein until it has been felt by the finger, and if he opens it, it must be narrow."
"And therefore, it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and attentive to all these causes."
"And then withdraw the lancet, but if the vein fills up at once, that is good. But if not, repeat this process in another part nearby, or even above it, with haste, provided there is no apostema."
"But if the location is inflamed or the condition worsens, then leave it for a day or two, do not tighten the bandage, lest it draw forth an ulcerated inflammation, nor take a bath."
"And repeat phlebotomy if necessary."
"If the opening is small and blood flows out subtly, and you fear that not enough blood will come out, then repeat the lancet in that same place according to rectitude and adjust the opening slightly, and do this with haste before the location becomes inflamed, for many people's locations become inflamed due to a small opening."
"But if the location is already inflamed, then do not repeat it there, for it will serve no purpose. Rather, pause on some part of the oil groove to ease the flow of blood." "And oil is better in this location than any other ointment."
"And similarly, administer an oil groove in every phlebotomy where veins are opened with the prohibition of blood flow, and they do this by applying treacle, alfalfa, and sage when pausing on one of those locations, for blood is subtilized and made to revolve when it is thick."
"If a large inflammation has arisen in the location of phlebotomy, which happens many times, especially to those who have not been bled before or whose vein opening was small, then apply a sponge soaked in water and salt, lightly scorched, over that place for an hour, for it will be dissolved."
"And this should be done immediately after the blood has flowed from that vein or another one."
"If a blackness or greenness remains in the location for several days, do not worry about it. But if you wish, apply some solution of aloe and myrrh, or some juice of mint, chamomile, or the like."
"And many times apostemes and elevations happen due to phlebotomy at the basilica."
"Then place your hand over that and if you still feel softness under your pressure, then that elevation is harmful and bad. And therefore be sure to apply some of what we have said there to prevent the flow of arterial blood."
"Prepare a plaster with something that has astringent properties to harden the area, then treat it with another cure until it is healed."
"It is necessary to draw blood from each person according to their quantity of vital force and the prevailing color above their blood."
"But if the blood is black, then let it flow until it becomes red; and similarly if you find it thick, then let it flow until it becomes subtiliated; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness has been diminished."
"And for someone who is full and robust and needs bloodletting at one time, the venipuncture should be widened and the incision made wide."
"And for someone who is cold, do the opposite of what was done for the previous person, and let them bleed in several sessions, with a narrow incision."
"It is better to make a transverse section of the vein when performing venipuncture than to prick it."
"And this method of bloodletting preserves against the flow of blood and injury to nerves, and it is safer and more laudable than bloodletting by width and length."
"And when syncope is about to occur during bloodletting, you should care for the patient beforehand with a little powder of millet seeds or simple sage infused in wine; and let out their blood in three or four sessions."
"And if it is coldness of temperament, then take before bloodletting a powder to infuse in a syrup of myrrh or in an aromatic syrup sweetened with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger and the like, or in a highly fragrant aromatic syrup."
"And if syncope occurs during bloodletting and it is due to the letting out of much blood, then you should give them to drink water of meat and highly fragrant syrup; and apply aromatics with aloe, and let them lie quietly on their side."
"And use the other causes that we have mentioned in the division of syncope by evacuation."
"And whoever wants to expand his veins and dissolve his blood according to the second method should let out blood from him with venipuncture for much evacuation, and the power of it is cold, which evacuates blood little by little, so it depends on the amount of power in consecutive days."
"But whoever wants to expand his veins and draw out his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then do so about seven or nine hours after the first venipuncture."
"But whoever wants to draw out blood from his body contrary to the direction that it declines, should have it expanded on the second or third day."
"But in that body in which there is little blood already let out and it is acrid and causes fever to occur, you should draw out blood from him in one session and draw out a large quantity of it, and amplify the aperture until syncope occurs; for you will have preserved all the conditions of bloodletting; and place your hand on his pulse before the body's blood, so that death does not happen instead of syncope, for many times this happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent."
"And when you want to release the veins and evacuate blood a second time, since the opening of the vein is closed and it is difficult to draw out blood, do not press with violence and curvature, but gently extend it; for this is very bad."
"Let him lie down again until he is phlebotomized a second time; or remove it with the acuteness of the bloodletting that has coagulated in the opening of the vein; or place upon it tyriaca alfarach, or sage, and press it gently until the blood flows out."
"And if the vein is inflamed, then leave that one alone and do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided."
"And if necessity provokes you to evacuate blood a second time, and there is no excuse for it, then draw blood from him on that spot or in the other arm in another vein."
"And the vein called 'funis of the arm' is bloodletted in place of the furca or the basilica when they are not available or concealed, for it is composed of those."
"And the quality of bloodletting from it is that the patient should immerse his hand in cold water until the fleshy part reddens and the vein appears manifestly."
"And it should be stretched out with a linen bandage with moderate tension; and bloodletting should be done according to the transverse line, not latitudinally nor according to the longitude."
"And let the aperture be wide and your bloodletting with which you bloodlet him be on the junction of his hand a little bit."
"And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse your hand in cold water again and let the blood run into the water until your necessity is satisfied."
"But if you are in the days of summer, then you are exempt from immersing your hand in cold water; and let the body's blood be placed in cold water during wintertime."
"And bloodletting from this vein is safer than from all other veins, for it has neither a pulsating vein nor nerves."
"And bloodletting from the little bag of the right hand is beneficial for diseases of the liver."
"And the quality of bloodletting from it is that you should stretch out the hand with linen or your hand, since you have placed it in cold water, until the vein is inflated and clearly demonstrated to view; and draw blood transversely from it; and if you should happen to miss it entirely, do not concern yourself about it."
"And take care that you do not extend your hand too much during bloodletting, for there are the nerves of these veins denuded of flesh."
"And appropriately immerse the hand in cold water and let the blood run into it, for if you do not immerse it in cold water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein and its flow is prohibited."
"And when blood has been extracted according to the quantity required, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not close suddenly; and do likewise in every straight vein."
"But the benefit of bloodletting from that of the left hand is that it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen."
"And do likewise in bloodletting from it as you have done with the other."
"And the benefit of bloodletting from the vein called sephena is for diseases that are in the parting of the limbs, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menses, and for diseases of the joints and ulcers of skin and old scars, and their like diseases."
"And the quality of bloodletting from it is that the patient should immerse his foot in cold water and make friction upon it until the veins appear clearly; and tie a bandage on the junction of the foot; and the place where the vein appears is behind the said foot, and it branches out into many ramifications on the front of the foot."
"And therefore draw blood from the most abundant branch or that which is nearest to the heel behind the aggregation of it; for it is better and safer."
"And if you draw blood from it on the face of the foot, then take care of the nerves that are close to it on the face of the foot, and make your bloodletting with that with which you would cut, namely transversely."
"And if you wish, perform a phlebotomy."
"But if the bleeding is forbidden; then immerse the patient's foot in cold water and let the blood flow into that container until it has stopped."
"And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then retry with the phlebotomy later in that area because it will have healed by now. And do not fear the patient's maliciousness but be wary of the nerves as we have said."
"And similarly act regarding the vein of the other foot accordingly."
"And concerning the sciatic vein, the location is roughly at the level of the heel on the posterior aspect of the leg."
"The benefit of phlebotomy for him is to ease his hip pain when it is due to hot blood."
"The quality of the phlebotomy for him is that the patient enters into a bath, and he quickly and strongly ties his left hip joint with a long thin bandage so that only that appears."
"And then when it appears, immediately phlebotomize that vein according to your ability and according to the transverse vein, and this is better. Or else cut that with an incision or dissect it with a section because the location of it has healed."
"And it is in many men not visible; and if you find that vein, but it does not appear at all to the touch, then phlebotomize some branch of it, and that is which appears on the back of the hand towards the said auricular and the annular bones."
"And beware of the nerves, and let flow as much blood as you wish."
"And then untie the ligation and pause over the site of phlebotomy until it stops flowing, because it heals quickly."
"Regarding venesection and its qualities for use."
"Wounds are of horn, or of iron, or of vein."
"And ventosing is done in two ways: one is by scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is ventosing without scarification."
"And this ventosing which is done without scarification is performed in two ways: with fire or without fire."
"And the locations on the body where ventosas are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen in number, of which the first one is placed near the bladder, which is the last section of the head; and the middle location of it, divided into two parts, is at the nape of the neck; and ventosas of the chin beneath the jawbone below the mouth; and ventosas of both shoulders; and ventosas of the hip bones, which are above the buttocks; and ventosas of both knees, which are on both thighs; and ventosas of both ankles; and ventosas of both elbows."
"And ventosing does not extract blood from the large veins, but only from the small ones scattered throughout the flesh, and for this reason it does not draw out the humor by that means, as is done by phlebotomy. And it is not necessary to perform ventosing with fire or without fire in any illness caused bypletion until the whole body has been evacuated."
"But if there is a need to provoke ventosing for an illness or continuous fever, we administer it at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time."
"And therefore, whenever there is such a cause in any of those cases, and especially if it is on the third or midday of the month, we command that ventosing be performed after two or three hours have passed since that time."
"And the benefit of ventosing on the nape is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and causes sinking into the eyes. But this should be accompanied by evacuation of the whole body."
"And this ventosing is in the place of phlebotomy on the cephalic vein."
"And it is not lawful to use it for one who has full flesh, or in whom there is catarrh, because it causes great harm."
"And therefore, one should not use it on the elderly, and on those whose heads have hot illnesses."
"But one who is continuous about it may cause forgetfulness. And therefore, you should command the ventosing to pause his hand a little at the beginning of the operation, fearing the generation of forgetfulness."
"But ventosing of alkehel (madder) is in the place of phlebotomy on the vein nigra and basilica: And therefore it causes asthma, and constriction of the lung, and splitting of the windpipe of the lung, and coughing, andpletion."
"And therefore, madder ventosing should be a little paced out, because if it is too sudden it causes weakness in the body and stomach."
"And the ventosing of alhagadhan (henbane) causes accidental pains in the head and inflammation of the eyes, migraine, suffocation, and pains in the roots of teeth. And it is in the place of phlebotomy on the basilica vein."
"And you should command the ventoser not to press his hand with the scarification so that he does not wound the artery and cause bleeding."
"And ventosing of the chin causes coldness in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and appearance of diseases that are in the mouth. And it is in the place of phlebotomy on those veins that are on the two lips."
"But ventosing that is performed on the two spatulas causes headache, which occurs due topletion of heat."
"And the ventosing of both sides of the temples causes benefit from phlebotomy on three veins: namely the basilica, nigra and cephalica. Because it attracts blood from all those veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins that are between the other thicker veins so that the attraction reaches the three veins."
"And command the ventoser not to proceed with scarification because the place is devoid of flesh, and too near are nerves and arteries."
"And it is a ventosing performed on the buttocks which causes hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcers nearby."
"And you should command the ventoser to perform a large ventosing with an earthen pot. Because that place requires strong suction; and few subtle attracted ventosings are performed, and it should be scarified with scarification."
"But ventosings on the fleshy camels [thighs] cause strong diminution by clear diminution. Because they attract blood from all over the body and cause old pains in the kidneys and belly, and stimulate menstruation, and cause botch and ademoma [hemorrhoids]. And they are in the place of phlebotomy on two saphenas, alarcochan, and two cavillas."
"However, they debilitate the whole body and cause fainting in many people."
"And from the benefits of alarcochan ventosing, the benefit is close to the benefit of two camber [thigh] ventosings."
"And the quality of the position of ventosings is that you pause the ventosing at the beginning, wait and suck moderately, and do not prolong the position of the ventosings, but rather pause them lightly and remain them carefully. And let the humors come to the place by gentle arrival." "And do not cease iterating this and continuing until you see the place already reddened, swollen, and the appearance of blood's hue."
"And then you should scarify and iterate suction little by little, and carefully consider the disposition of the body."
"Because that person who has tender flesh and few pores should be scarified once only, not more than that. Because the place may become ulcerated."
"And command the ventoser to enlarge the scarification a little and press gently, and temper the suction with ease."
"And if the blood is thickened then you should scarify twice. Because in the first time the way of subtilization of blood and its wateriness is opened." "But in the second one, the extraction of thick blood is obtained through scrutinization."
"And if the blood is very fetid then there should be no scarification in the third time so that it can succeed [the ventosing]."
"Finally, when we want to extract blood little by little or a little, one scarification is enough for us."
"And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we should scarify with many scarifications. And if we know that the blood is thick then we should scarify by pressing scarification."
"And the term of scarification means only the depth of the skin."
If the chain is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are narrow, make sure the places for cups are anointed leniently, opened, loosened; if it is summer time, thus with oil of aloe or violet oil, or oil of crushed walnuts, or oil of chamomile flowers; but if it is winter time, then use narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or blood oil, and those like these.
If the swelling is large and fiery, then use oil of amaranth, or balm oil, or aloe oil, or pennyroyal oil, and those like these.
But if the cupping glass has wide pores of tender flesh, then it is necessary to prohibit oil, and for this one the places of cups near the cupping should be washed with warm or hot water of solatrum, or chamomile water, or borages water, and similar things.
And for him whose blood is of much humidity, wash his cups with sour wine or myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for him whose swellings are large, wash his cups with old wine or water of sanicle, which is marjoram, or decoction of fennel, or chamomile, and those like these.
And it is necessary that cupping should be guarded beforehand from bathing. It is also necessary that it should be administered near immersion in the bath for one or two hours, and it is necessary that he should sleep near cupping.
"What is this: That it is necessary to guard the person being cupped before cupping."
At the beginning you should consider: if the one doing the cupping or bleeding is choleric and vents upon hot blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling causes, such as sour cherries, endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secanolbin, and julep, and those like these.
And pause their foods thus as polenta, and fowl meats roasted, and foods seasoned with sour things, and those like these.
And for him whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he should drink hippocrates syrup of almonds or hippocrates syrup of fennel seeds and sumac, and fragrant wine better between young and old; and that he be commanded to use scarcity of drinking and eating, and pause foods of polenta and canabis and fish and small birds and pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bleeding he should drink more than eat food.
And it is necessary that in some hours certain people should be given tyriaca altharac or musk medicaments, such as musk or muscated wine before cupping or bleeding or near them, so that the principal members may be comforted and the blood subtilized; but they should not take that scalding.
Mark those cupping glasses that are without scarification as being on the liver, and on the spleen and podagra, and on the navel, in the joints of the loins, and on the anus, because these members do not suffer scarification upon them.
And it is not desired by those except for attraction of blood from the member, thus as pause cupping on the breast in illness of flux of blood from the nose; and they use that for the dissolution of cold ventosity undissolved from the member, such as pause on the belly and navel; because that rarifies the member and scalds it and removes pain because it resolves ventosity.
And already cupping is paused on the nostrils when there occurs in their obstruction or phlegm because by virtue of strong attraction it draws out obstruction and expels phlegm from its place.
Similarly, it does when paused on the liver and spleen to draw forth ventosity that is enclosed within them.
These cupping glasses are administered with suction alone; some times they are administered with fire.
Some times they are administered full of cold water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is why the cupping glass should be filled and made larger, that is cold water alone or water in which herbs are cooked according to what is convenient.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire: the width of the mouth should be as we have formed it; and the quantity of the preon should be the most of a palm; and it should be on the side of him, in the place that is in the circuit, the most of him, the traction should be little according to the quantity that enters him through a needle.
It should be made of red earthenware or glass, with thick rims, lion's teeth, edges and smooth for that it does not wound the member through its position on him.
And in the middle of it a transverse wick of earthenware or iron should be placed on which a candle is paused with fire.
And already this cupping glass should be much larger than that which we have recommended; and the little one less according to the illnesses and the state of him who uses it.
The cupping glasses of children and weak ones are other than those of youths and strong-bodied people.
The quality of pausing this cupping glass with fire on the member is that it extends the mecha with fire of wood decently before a small candle of wax, and pauses that on the middle of the transverse wick in the middle place of the cupping glass, so that the fire may be kindled around the partition of the sides so that not the skin of the sick person may be burned.
And soon it should be paused on the member of the sick person, and the said thread should be on the traction that we have said, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not desire to pull the thread, then the vapor will rise over that traction, at that time the cupping glass will soon be dissolved.
And then light the wick according to its manner, and renew it if necessary.
But in cupping glass that is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, in which there is no transverse wick nor traction, and it is not administered except that it should be filled with water, and it is paused on the member alone.
And this is its shape: This cupping glass the larger it is, the better, for that it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter XCVII
Blood-letting cups are not administered on large members except on those in which the position of cupping glasses is not possible because of their smallness, such as the throat and gums and similar ones; or on member denuded of flesh, such as the thigh and nose and theirs.
And the quality of using them is that if found blood-sucking leeches that are in the waters of putridity clean.
And soon they should be placed day and night in putrid water until they are hungry, and that no cause remains in the belly of them; and soon the body should be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping glasses; and soon rub the member of the sick person until it becomes red; and soon pause them on him.
And when they are full and satisfied.
And if it is possible to draw out the matter with cupping glass, it is better in judgment.
But if not, then wash the place with sour wine and soon with much water; and let it be rubbed and expressed.
And if the course of blood is prolonged because of the wound of the blood-sucking leeches, and it becomes red, then you should put a cloth soaked in hot water on him so that the bleeding may be stopped.
"When blood is increased, then powdered vitriol trisulfate is applied, or poultices and similar causes of obstruction are used until the bleeding is stopped; or raw mung beans may be placed on the spot."
"And also, if necessary, the application of leeches for bloodletting should be repeated when possible.
"And if the application of leeches by suspension is prohibited, then the spot should be rubbed with fresh blood or a needle inserted into it until some blood flows out; and immediately afterward compresses should be applied, because those who feel any blood flowing at once should adhere to them."
"And when you want them to stop, powder a little alum Trisulfate or salt or ash on them, and immediately they will cease."
Here begins the prologue of the third part: On restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine, and it concerns the reduction (setting) of fractures and accidental luxations of bones.
"Oh son, know that those who boast about this chapter are foolish doctors and common folk, and those who at any time have not regarded the books of the Ancients nor read even one letter of them."
"And because of this, the manner of practicing the science in our land has become private; and I myself am not oblivious at all to this during this good time. And I have not added what follows except out of respect for the length of reading the books of the Ancients and study on their understanding, so that I might extract the knowledge from them."
"And moreover, I direct this to experiment and study throughout the length of my life."
"And I have already described in this chapter everything that my knowledge contains and on which my experiment has passed; because here I have abbreviated it for you others and delivered it from the occupation of lengthening. And I have abbreviated it to the utmost degree of abbreviation, and I have demonstrated it to the utmost degree of declaration, and I have formed many forms of the instruments that are administered in this one, as is done by addition of declaration, just as I have done in two preceding chapters."
Chapter One
"Summary and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from which a preamble is necessary before we begin to remember the members that have been broken and their different types." "Therefore, I will begin this chapter by stating the summaries of what will be said, and the chapters that require understanding at the beginning for those who wish to master this art and remain firm on its truth; namely you and others who are eager to know or learn this noble art."
"And so I say that when an accident occurs such as a fracture or dislocation or torsion or compression, it is necessary at the beginning to apply flebotomy or bleeding to that person, or sometimes both, if there is no prohibition of any cause such as debility of power, or if he is an infant or old in decrepitude, or during times of great heat or cold."
"And moreover, let him be content with his food of fresh herbs and the flesh of birds and crabs; and let him prohibit heavy meats and filling foods, and all foods that fill the veins with blood, so that you may be sure of a safe cauterization, and not fear an effusion of matter to the member."
"And then return the patient to his first regimen, with which he is accustomed to live."
"And then, when the broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the patient be fed nutritious foods of abundant and heavy nutrition with viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, rice, chickpeas, eggs, beef, butter, fresh cheese, thick wine, and similar foods. And with this regimen, coagulation of the fracture occurs more gently and quickly."
"And know that bones are more brittle in men who are in the first age of life and in the decrepit, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the drying out of their members."
"But nevertheless, nature adjusts and unites the bone that has been broken from any cause similar to glue with which it adheres and strengthens it until they are simultaneously conglutinated and united together, until the end of its strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning until no cause prohibits it from its work."
"And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the foods of the patient, foods in which there is gravity and viscosity and heaviness, as we have said."
"And know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all other members are diverse among themselves."
"And I come with remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinguished from the others."
"Great diversity even within the same fracture." "And the species of bone fractures are diversified among themselves; for there is an arched fracture that occurs sometimes in stars; and sometimes there is a fracture according to the length of the bone: these are the fractures of the lower jaw, upper jaw, and additional proceeding and non-proceeding ones, such as those made when preparing a pen to write, and there is a fracture with swelling and disruption at the head, and there is a small fissure-like fracture."
"And for each species there will be its own treatment in the restoration of it, according to what will come later distinguished in its places."
"Many signs of bone fracture." "And for this reason a fracture of the bone is known: its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that of it when you compress it with your hand."
"And then when there is no twisting apparent in the place nor is it done by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient finds little pain, then there is no fracture here."
"It may happen that there is only slight twisting or a small fissure, so it is not necessary to move it with your hand and compress all of it. Rather, pause on this place with the medicines about which remembrance will come at that moment which is appropriate for the place; and especially let there be strictness by means of subtle constriction."
"And know that the place when it is arched and not split into two or other than those that occur in the jawbone, if all the parts of its companion are removed, then you should begin from your own hour to rectify it having applied a cauterizing plaster to it."
"And if a cauterizing plaster is applied to it, then leave it alone for several days until the plaster stops. And then move it according to each manner that you are able by raising and moving."
"And know that the restoration and alignment of it is easier than for the bone in which a jawbone fracture has occurred."
"And then strengthen it according to what will come later its remembrance."
"But if it is the jawbone, then it will be necessary to extend this broken member from both parts, if it is the hand or foot. Or with your hand if the member is small. Or with two ropes or one rope and your hand."
"And let your position be such that you place the affected limb according to its natural shape, while ensuring that the parts are extended as much as possible. Then proceed to remove any excess fluid in accordance with each manner that you may find in their places."
"And be careful according to your skill that you do not harm the patient through your operation of their limbs or cause them any injury. And proceed according to your ability, applying one bone with another according to their best forms."
"And see that at this opportune moment you touch the patient with your hand gently and if there should be any other cause, correct it and reduce it according to the amount of your strength."
"And avoid violent extension and compression, as do many foolish physicians who often cause an abscess to form through their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen many times."
"And the quality of strapping for a tranquil limb is what I recommend; because you should know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and greatness, and in their form."
"And for those that are small, such as the arm, hand, and foot, and similar ones, then the wrapping should be made of very soft cloth."
"But those that are large, such as the thigh or calf, should have a firm and hard wrapping; because the tight wrapping connects the limb strongly on all sides with equal strapping, so that there is no gap."
"And first, begin your evacuation of the hematoma by placing suitable linen on that location for three or four wrappings according to the size of the limb. And bind it tightly with your hand using the linen."
"And then, above that at the beginning of the location of the fracture, bind again with another wrapping so that your previous strapping is not loosened from where you have strapped the fracture." "And then extend your wrapping of the location of the fracture a little further downwards and slowly loosen the strapping a bit at a time until you reach its upper part."
"And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the location of the fracture with a wrapping, and then proceed upwards with the wrappings away from the fracture; and your operation should be in the tightening of the wrapping and loosening of it according to what has been said regarding the first wrapping."
"And then insert between the wrappings a wisp of wool or cloth with which to correct any twisting of the fracture if there is any twisting in it. And if not, do not place anything in it for any reason."
"And then place another bandage on top and bind it gently over these wrappings with proper care, if there is no inflammation or abscess in that location. And if there is an inflammation or abscess, then place the bandage over what covers the inflammation or sits on the abscess; and leave it for a few days. And then you should tighten the wrappings."
"And the splints should be made of soft, fine-grained cane, well-formed; or they may be made of hazelwood; or branches of palm tree. And each splint should be of this width and shape:"
"However, the splint placed over that fracture should be thicker and broader than the other splints, and its length should correspond to the size of the limb. And then bind over the splints with another bandage according to your first strapping. And then tie it gently on top according to what has been said about strapping; and ensure that your strapping is according to the location of the fracture, and the further away from the fracture, the looser should be the strapping."
"And the threads should be soft and fine in thickness. And if the threads are thick, as I have seen some people do when they use cords of twisted flax, it is a great mistake because it makes the strapping uneven."
"And very fine threads are not suitable either because you will not be able to tighten as much with them as you want. And there should not be less than a finger's width between splint and splint."
"And if the patient feels discomfort at the ends of the splints near the strapping, then place some wool or lamb's wool between them until it ceases to cause injury."
"Some fractures are with displacement, such as in the elbow joint, and this happens occasionally. I have spoken about this in Chapter II on Particularities."
"And know that not every fractured limb should be immobilized with splints on the first day. The reason for this is that when a limb is large, it does not require splints to be placed over it immediately, but rather after 2, 3 or more days depending on your assurance of whether an abscess will form."
"And this is the method used by plasterers with which the Ancients used to treat fractures and dislocations and twists."
"Method of plastering commonly used for treating fractures, suitable especially for complicated cases and mainly for women and children because it does not allow heat or friction. And this is how you should proceed: Take some powder from the mill, which is the bran left behind after sifting the flour."
"And then sieve it with album of egg whites and make a paste that is neither too thick nor too thin. And then use this."
"Another method of plastering for treating fractures and dislocations and twists: Take assafetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and millet flour, which is ground from wild grains; mix them together with mirrh and aloe in equal parts. Then sift everything thoroughly and combine it with chicory water or egg whites if the condition is scalded. And then use this plaster because it has a good composition and treats lightly fractured bones well. It benefits many people."
"Another method of plastering that is suitable for treating fractures and twists: Take millet flour and white wheat flour in equal parts, 10 drachms each; mirrh and aloe, one-half drachm each; acacia, six drachms; and bol armenic, twenty drachms."
"All these are ground together in a mortar with great care and sifted. Then they are mixed with water or egg whites and applied."
"Method of plastering for joints and bones separated from their places, which cause pain in those areas and for cracks and fractures: Take raw wool, immerse it in sour wine and cooked oil, and place it over the location."
"But this plaster is not useful for restoring broken bones or drawing out pus. It only serves to relieve pain."
"Another method of plastering that restores broken bones: Take fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; combine them together and make a plaster from them."
"A plaster for healing and treating fractures of the bone and to resolve any remaining abscess: take roots of mallow, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, flour from oats, all in equal parts; all ground together and cooked with wine. If the member is not cut."
"If it is scratched, then wash it with cold water or with water from a bunch of celery, and use it."
"Another plaster for healing abscesses that is stronger than the first one, when an abscess occurs during the restoration of a member: take roots of mallow and its seeds, and balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all ground together; all are ground together and cooked with water from walnut trees or with sweet water or with wine, all according to the quality of the member and the restfulness of its color."
"But Hippocrates does not specify in his book that it should be placed on the member when the restoration of it is broken, but rather a plaster made from wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it should be mixed between thickness and fineness."
"However, Galen says that they should be placed on the broken member during its restoration and he gives as causes of drying with little heat, like myrrh, incense and similar things, those same causes which we have recommended."
"A way to make a plaster that relieves injury and pain: take ground frankincense, aloes, peeled human skin or chicken feathers, mallow, salt, all ground together; they should be sieved and sifted and cooked, and made into a plaster."
"However, the quantity that should remain on the bandage and which should be gradually removed is this: if there is no pain or swelling nor is the bone broken, then you do not need to remove it for many days." "And if pain or swelling or inflammation occurs in the place, then you should immediately remove it and discard that plaster."
"And take a cloth and wet sea sponge, and immerse that in hot water, and with it wash the inner part of the place until that swelling has ceased and the pain has been soothed; and then let the member rest for an hour; and then apply over it clean wool immersed in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and wrap it around it for a night so that you are sure of cauterizing the abscess and that the inflammation of the member is removed and the pain from it has gone away."
"And then apply that fine dressing and plaster again in small quantity and do not tighten too much with your first binding, but rather bind it more subtly until it is healed."
"And if at some point the abscess and heat and pain and inflammation and all accidents have been removed entirely, and you need plaster and binding again, then you should apply it in the same way as you did at the beginning."
"And if no cause of those that we have mentioned occurs to the member, then you should not remove it but rather leave it on for three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all of this is left on for twenty days according to how it appears to you through the removal of the bandage from the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and approaches the coagulation of the flesh over it."
"And then tighten the binding more than your first binding all around, and adjust it according to the swelling of the wound of the sick person, as it is recalled."
"But if you see that the place of the fracture has dried out and become shriveled more than it should, then you should know that nourishment is prohibited from coming to that place; therefore, immerse that in hot water every time you remove it, and do this for all three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation the member will receive nourishment, and it will be healed easily."
"But what foolish restorers of the fracture of the member do again when it is not restored at first, according to what is necessary, and is restored crookedly, is error according to their operation, great fear, and if they had been right this would have enlarged the Ancients in their books and taught with that operated."
"And I have not seen any of them do this, so it is more reasonable that they should not do it."
"Chapter according to: On fracture when it occurs in the head."
"The kinds of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and their causes are distinct. For some fractures happen by percussion of a blow, and the percussion leaves all the bone inside the head, which is left behind like the wood in the tree trunk, and for this reason this species is called 'asciacion.' Or the blow cuts through some part of the bone, cutting into the flesh but not passing through to the other side. This species of fracture is called 'levation absolute.'"
"And the plague of these two fractures is great or little, and of fracture that is contusion or abrasion. The cause of it is percussion with heavy blow or impact on stone, and things similar to them."
"And this fracture is penetrating and approaches the periosteum, which is left behind with the bone, or it is on the surface of the bone. The plague of these two fractures is great or little."
"And there is fracture that is jammed into the bone in the thinness of a hair's breadth, and for this reason this species is called 'capillary fracture.'"
"And it is fracture that happens by impact or blow of stone and things similar to it, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter into its parts from within, as happens to the wings of birds when they suffer percussion and their bones are driven in from within."
"And this happens especially in the bodies of moist bones, like those of elephants' heads."
"And therefore for all these species of fractures sought after, they are painful like separated stars." "And I will come back with remembrance of all this in its place."
"And you should know all these species of fracture by detection of those and by inquiry about them with touch and inspection of rotting flesh that is over them."
"And the capillary species is known by this: it is discovered on the bone and cleaned, and touched with a thread, because the fracture appears black."
"But in the treatment of fracture, first consider the accidents of the illness. And then if you see through those accidents that it signifies by clear indication about fear, like vomiting from cholera and extension and loss of consciousness and fainting and syncope and acute fever and discharge of eyes and redness of them and similar things to these in the major dispositions, it is not cured."
"And if you see accidents that are not usual, then hope for health, and then begin the treatment of it."
"And this is because if the plague has come to you at first as it was and that happened in winter days, then you should study the inspection of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition."
"And if it is in summer days, then you should have the bone inspected on the seventh day, having that which is left behind with the bone corrupted by the periosteum, and let those accidents happen that we have recommended."
"And if the fracture has already reached the periosteum covering the brain and is with small partitions and attrition, then you should incise the committed and crushed partition, according to what I will recommend to you."
"It is necessary that you raise the head of the sick person gently, uncover it according to custom and manner as much as possible in accordance with the shape of the plague, and that it be light upon the sick."
"If you detect the swelling, fluxes of blood or hard abscess, then you should pause and prepare what is necessary, and if possible, place cloths infused in wine and rose oil on it, and let the swelling rest until the abscess has sedated, being sure that there are no fluxes of blood; and immediately begin with the circumcision of the swelling and its ablation."
"This is according to one or two methods of operation."
"And the first method is that you cut the swelling with a straight incision thus: And immediately administer another incision somewhat further on than this one..."
"And immediately administer another incision further yet."
"And it is necessary that you make diverse incisions of various lengths, some of which are further apart than others; and some of them are shorter than others and sharp at their ends, made with a knife or scalpel."
"And use them with ease in percussion upon the incisions so that you do not wound the head and harm him."
"And if the swelling is hard and tough, then you should first make a perforation around it before administering the incisions with the tweezers, which are called non-penetrating tweezers."
"And they are not called thus because those do not reach the end of the swelling near where it is thickest, so that the tweezers have a rounded extremity or margin over that which leaves the head sharp like a margin or small circle, which should be submerged and pass through the thickness of the swelling."
"And it is convenient that you take from these tweezers a large number of them, each one convenient for the thickness of each swelling, so that there may be present in all swellings of the brain an extremity which is sharp, and its quantity in length and breadth will be according to the quantity of thickness of this swelling."
"And this is the manner of three kinds of tweezers: The quality of perforating around the broken swelling is that you place the tweezer on the swelling and turn it with your fingers until you know that the swelling has been penetrated; and immediately move the tweezer to another place, and pause when there is between everything the quantity of the thickness of a ray or environ."
