"And immediately incise with incisions that which is between each tract of the swelling, and make it as subtle as possible by means of levitation, as we have said before, until you eradicate the swelling, or with your hand, or with some other cause through certain instruments which you will have prepared for this purpose, such as forceps and delicate tweezers."
"And it is necessary that you guard with all caution that the incisions do not touch any cause of the pharynx."
"And then when you have circumcised the swelling with perforations, and if it is separate from the pharynx if it is continuous with it, you will extract it, taking care to raise and separate it in the thickness of it from that which is in the swelling with another instrument similar to incisions, but nevertheless it should be more thin and subtle than other incisions."
"And if any fragment of the little bones remains and sticks out, then take it with ease with that which you have to use among the instruments; and immediately cure the wound with stitches and dressings which we have mentioned."
"And the other manner of operation is light and distant from fear, which is praised by Galen and Avicenna for great praise; and this is their speech: It is necessary for you at the beginning that you begin to uncover the fracture of the bone in the place where it is most violent and manifest, until when you have uncovered that fracture and paused beside it, you will place the extremity of this lent and gentle incision of which this is the form:"
"And it is that the lenticular incision is long and does not incise any cause, and the acute incision is of it on both sides forward in length, as times are; so that the lenticular incision is adapted to the pharynx with the face of the incident acute bone."
"And then strike with a small hammer on the incision with one blow so that you incise all the bone with ease as it revolves, and you are sure of the paniculus that does not occur in it accidentally that you fear in any manner, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly he was asleep."
"Then if any cause is attached to the bone of the paniculus in some places of the bone, incise it with the extremity of this lenticular incision and detach it from it with ease because it has been detached from it without harm and without fear."
"And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the paniculus, and that which is cut off or remains on the bone is its face, and remains in it thickness and subtle adherences, then you should raise this thickness and eradicate those adherences with delicate razors by means of which you have prepared a number of different quantities so that you can use them in all places the most convenient for them and the most upright according to what you perceive about your operation on that broken bone and its figure."
"And you should administer in your operation with which you scrape the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors, and immediately use the most subtle of them, and do not cease to do this continuously until you arrive at administering the thinnest and the most subtle of all of them."
"But in each of the other little capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer cure according to what you perceive regarding their correction."
"And this is because it is not agreed upon by all those who have studied that art." "And we have shown in large fractures regarding this that was mentioned above."
"And if the paniculus is torn when you eradicate the bone, then you should take cloth according to the quantity of the wound and immerse it in rose oil and place it on the opening of the wound; and immediately take another folded or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place it on the first cloth and do this as lightly as possible so that you do not press the pharynx and immediately apply a wide bandage, and do not stretch it except by the quantity that the wound has alone."
"And leave it with this cure for one or two days until you are sure of the formation of cauterization; and then untie it and immediately apply medicines with which there is superfluous dryness, such as rose water and barley flour, powder of hyssop and aristolochia, and similar things to them."
"And it is that you make these medicines into powder that is finely powdered on the wound as they are dry; and generally, apply to it every medicine with the property of cleaning without biting."
"And guard yourself according to your skill that the wound in your treatment is clean and not in its dirtiness of dressing or other; nor do you allow virulence to gather on it, for when it settles on the brain's paniculus, it corrupts it and makes it putrefy, and immediately brings about severe illness."
"And it happens in the pharynx of the head, especially when the bone is scraped and most when its cure is neglected, and blackness appears on its surface; therefore consider immediately if it happens to the patient the accidents that we have mentioned, you would know that they are fatal without doubt."
If blackness occurs only from the medicine applied above and it is due to that medicine causing this blackness, then you should take some honey and three parts of rose oil, line a cloth with them, place it on the head, and cure it with appropriate remedies until it is healed.
Here comes another sentence:
"On restoring a crushed nose."
"You should know that it is not broken but rather two fractures occurring simultaneously and then healing, or one of those injuries seen sometimes, for which reason in the nose there are two bones, since the fracture leaves behind cartilage, not bone, and therefore what happens here is merely a bruise with tortuosity and sinuosity."
"But if one of the two fractures in the nose is broken, then you must insert your little finger into its nasal cavity and use it to push back that fracture with your thumb inside and index finger outside until the nose returns to its natural shape." "And may you accomplish this with ease, but guard against causing any harm to that person in your operation."
"And if the fracture is in the previously healed part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the tip of a needle with a small thickness."
"And if there are fractures on both sides, then proceed as follows:"
"And strive for restoration of it on the first day following the fracture, if possible; otherwise, you should apply a poultice of boiled linseed or oil, as is appropriate, to avoid an abscess."
"And appropriate to the fracture, insert a wick of cloth into the tip of the nose if it is on one side, or two wicks if it is on both sides, and make them thick enough that they fill the space in the nose."
"And some of the ancient restorers say that the wicks should be wound at the edges and changed daily, but this is not seen by me. However, it is necessary that the wick be wrapped in album honey comfits with powdered myrrh; and appropriate that the wick remains until the bone is firm and the cartilage has hardened."
"And sometimes, instead of wicks, pieces of linen cloth are inserted into the nose, which can cause an accumulation of pus to form and be wrapped in linen. This is especially true if the fracture of the nose is very violent and the patient cannot breathe."
"And this cause is not necessary for you; do as you wish, either use wicks or cloths."
"And if there occurs another abscess, plaster the nose with wax or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon."
"But if no abscess occurs, then you should plaster it externally with a mixture of barley flour and powdered comfits of iris with honey; appropriate to pause on it with linen bandages, and do not touch the nose for any reason."
"But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, then you should split them open and remove them using appropriate instruments; appropriate to suture the wound and heal it with what binds and consolidates, and apply appropriate plasters."
"And if inflammation occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with wicks and insert lead cannulae until it is completely healed."
Chapter IV
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is broken.
When the lower jawbone is broken and there is no inflammation in the fracture, observe whether it is broken only externally, not in two pieces and curved at the ends inside; then its recognition will be easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left jawbone, then insert the right index finger; and press gently with that finger on the prominence of the fracture inside with facility at the edges outside, using your other hand to hold the edge of the bone outside; do this carefully.
And if the fracture of the jawbone is already separated into two pieces, then you should apply extension to both parts according to a straight line until it is possible to achieve reduction.
And if there is commotion of teeth with disruption, then strengthen those that you expect to break with gold or silver wire or thread; appropriate to plaster the broken jawbone with wax and also plaster it externally with doubled cloth and plaster it externally with a large linen bandage carefully done, or a splint of bone along the length of the jawbone; appropriate to bind it above according to how you have prepared the bandage and what is convenient for its application, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to rest and remain quiet, and avoid drinking hot liquids.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in some way, then wait and remove it on the third day; appropriate to correct what is altered about it and plaster it with powdered myrrh, honey, or barley flour, since you will have removed the wax from it, and apply a linen bandage over the plaster.
And then with this annexed and properly adjusted plaster on it, if the position of the bone is not altered, leave it in place until it is healed and strengthened; for many times this fracture heals in three weeks.
And if another time an abscess occurs, then apply what has been said many times in its seduction until it is healed and the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with inflammation, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or displaced; then be careful to remove those displacements using what is appropriate for that purpose among the instruments.
And if the opening of the inflammation is narrow, then you should dilate it with a trocar according to your need.
And appropriate when you have removed those displacements and if there remains no remainder from them, then close the opening of the inflammation if it is wide; and if not, plaster that with one of the plasters that are suitable for that purpose and heal it until it is healed.
Chapter V
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Many times the collarbone is broken at its anterior part near the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it may be broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without displacements; and this is easy to restore. Or it may be such that displacements occur in it, and this is difficult to restore. Or it may be such that the fracture is with inflammation.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without inflammation, is for you to have two assistants present, so that one holds the collarbone with forceps and follows the broken bone, and the other extends the neck at that part.
And appropriate to align the fracture with your fingers until it has been properly shaped according to what is necessary, and there remains neither prominence nor concavity.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a pad of cloth or wool on the patient's chin according to its size based on your need; elevate and extend the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand while aligning the fracture properly.
And if you cannot draw the end of the collarbone to its external part because it has been pushed forward towards the sternum, then it is necessary for the patient to be seated upright on his neck and place a large folded pad over that muscle.
And the assistant presses on that muscle at its external part until the bone of the collarbone is elevated and brought back to its normal position; then you should correct the fracture and align it with your fingers.
"If you feel pulsations in the collarbone and they are disturbing, then it is necessary to cut over that area and remove those pulsations with ease."
"And if it is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision on that area with one of the incising knives that you have prepared for this purpose, since you had already placed aside the collarbone, which serves as a lever; and it is a tool made of wood or iron, whose shape is thus:"
"And it resembles a spatula, the concave side of which corresponds to this shape; and its width should be according to your needs, depending on the size of the bone or the thickness of it."
"But its length should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; it should have two extremities thus, one of which is wide and the other is narrow."
"And if the disruption of the wound that you have incised when you extracted the pulsation from the bone is large, and you are certain that it will heal without infection, then you should approximate the two lips of the wound with suturing."
"And if the disruption is small, or you fear infection, then you should fill the wound with dressings and powders according to the quantity of the opening of the wound."
"And if an abscess occurs, then take a cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it on that area."
"And all of that, that the steel not be placed on top of the broken bone; and also so that you may see the injury every day, when the bandage becomes moist or you see that the steel is already loosened, then you should correct this and tighten the bandage."
"And also place the sleeping of the injured on top of his neck, and place it somewhat away from his side at night; so that with that the adjutors of him are elevated from the side of him, and therefore the fracture of the clavicle is elevated due to the elevation of the muscle; or the arm of him is tied to his neck."
"And do not remove the bandage until after twelve days if there is no swelling or abscess in that location."
"And also renew the plaster if you see the face of him become moist from that, and adjust it in the compression and restore that until it is restored and the fracture of the clavicle has coagulated."
"And it is strengthened and comforted in about twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it can be done in less time than that."
**Chapter VI.**
"For the restoration of a shoulder fracture."
"The spatula is broken at its lateral side and not completely but only on its edges."
"And therefore when it is broken at its middle side — and it is broken in the middle of it — and not known except by touch, then according to the figure of the fracture forcefully prepare it and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way that you can."
"And appropriately pause over the location of powdered chalk with egg white and cover it with linen; and also pause with doubled cloth powder; and also pause over the selected side of the subtle table according to the quantity of the entire spatula or a little more."
"But if you leave the selected side with concavity in some of the locations of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with linen and appropriately press the selected side evenly."
"And also strengthen it from above with a long bandage with appropriate compression and with firmness of the selected side so that it is not displaced from its location."
"And check the bandage every day; and whenever it becomes softened, apply another layer and strengthen it and press the selected side if it has become displaced from its location."
"And apply the plaster over its own side."
"And the bandage is restored in 20 days or 25 days."
"And therefore when this number is completed, then you dissolve the bandage, and be assured that it is of those bones from which the beginning was not feared nor their dissolution."
"And if you proceed from the location of the bone of the elbow and leave the upper arm, then incise over it, evacuate it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone concerning the removal of an abscess if one should occur here."
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the foot.
"The feet sometimes break in the middle of the body, but this is less common. The extremities are more often lame and shattered."
"When the middle of the body is broken by accident, it declines towards the parts within and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood. The fracture is evident in the cavity of the bone and cannot be hidden from sensation."
"And the restoration of it is that the sick person should be laid upon their back on a couch; and between the shoulders of them let there be placed a cushion. And gently press upon the muscles of them, and adjust the bones of them with the hands of two persons, handling the operation with subtilty, and adjusting according to all manner of way that is possible and convenient, until the figure of the bone returneth again into its proper place."
"And then apply plaster and lint, and place them on a subtle table of saunders or of chalk, and let them lie in levitation until you wrap them in cloth. And then handle that upon the broken bone with subtilty, taking care that it be not moved. And sometimes bind it around the back according to its roundness, and strengthen that with decent straitness; and always consider the bandage at every hour, and when it is softened, strengthen it."
"And if there is occasion to remove that bandage through breaking or pressing, pain, swelling, or apostume happening in the place, then thou shalt cut and loose that, and eradicate the plaster, and rectify whatsoever happened thereby with manner of cure thereof. And then again reduce the plaster, sometimes upon the face; and adjust the straitness until it be whole."
Chapter VIII
"Of the restoration of the ribs when they are broken."
"Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in large spaces that lie along the back, and at the extremities of their ends, no injury occurs except for this one because they are cartilage." "You will recognize it by feeling during its inquiry with your fingers."
"And the restoration should be done by setting the fracture with your fingers according to the most possible manner, ensuring that it is well aligned depending on what is necessary, and then apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel if needed."
"And if the rib fracture is displaced inward, then it causes violent pain and puncture, like the puncture that occurs in those who have coughed up blood, because the bone pierces the pleura. It also causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood; this is difficult to treat."
"And already the Ancients knew how to treat it with many devices."
"And they said that one should pause from eating for the duration of the illness that causes inflammation and windiness, so that the abdomen may be inflated and extended, and thus the fracture may be pushed outward."
"But we should not do this because it could easily cause an abscess to form if it is not done carefully. And if one has already formed, then it would be too late."
"And some of them said that one should pause from the windiness and then suck it with force. This is similar reasoning, but however it is feared that the windiness drives the impurities to the place through weakness."
"And some of them said: one should cover the place with wool that has already been immersed in hot oil, and then pause while powders are placed between the ribs until it is full. Then wrap it according to its roundness; and finally treat the illness with food and medicine for pleurisy."
"And if the illness causes violent constriction that cannot be endured, and the bone pierces the pleura with painful puncture, then one fears for the illness. Therefore, it is necessary to cut over the place, uncovering the broken rib, and then place on top of that instrument which retains the cyathiscus, whose memory is recalled earlier, in order to facilitate removing the bone and extracting it."
"And then join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suturing, and treat it with plasters until it heals."
"And if an abscess forms inside, then you can also apply powdered rose oil and pause over the place. And treat the illness with what is appropriate for an internal abscess."
"And let him be propped up on pillows while he lies in bed until he is healed."
Chapter IX
On the Restoration of Fractures of the Spine and Neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and sometimes they cause attrition there, and similarly with the spines of the back. "And when this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider this: if the sinews are already softened, stunned, deadened, and cannot be moved or extended or stretched, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle they do not feel pain, then you know that it will not heal, and in such cases it is mortal."
"And if they can be moved and feel constriction with your fingers or puncture, then you know that the nape of the neck is safe and the illness will be cured with treatment."
"And if it seems similar to fractures of the spines of the neck, then consider their weight."
"And then if you find that they are already softened like the sinews discussed earlier, and moreover when he lies on his back he feels windiness and expulsion without his will, and sometimes he is propped up on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and do not attempt treatment for it."
"But if none of these causes are found, then the cause is better and easier to treat."
"And the treatment for it is to apply hot compresses made with powdered rose oil or hot poultices of wheat flour and honey; pause over that place several times a day until the hard abscess is sedated."
"And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and stretch it over that place with linen; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and not to sleep on the part where he feels pain until he is healed."
"And if you find deviances or some other cause that has already separated from it, then you must cut over the spine and remove that bone; and moreover join the two lips of the wound with suturing if it is large; and treat it with some medication for inflammation until it heals."
"But if the end of the bone is broken off, and it is the end of the coccyx, then you must insert your left hand's little finger into the anus, and grasp the broken bone with your other hand according to what is possible for balance's sake; and moreover pause over that place with plaster and bandage if necessary, and also stretch it."
"And if you feel deviant breaks in it, then make an incision over that place, remove that, and treat the wound with the treatment that has been mentioned until it heals."
Chapter X.
"Of the restoration of health."
"The bones of the thighs are often broken, and if they break, the fracture of them is but a communication in the extremities thereof and are inserted in the longitude and decline into the interior parts; and it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the limb is stunned when it is fractured."
"And the restoration of it is that you stretch your hand over that which is above the fracture which is its figure."
"And if the fracture is only in the extremity thereof, then you shall join it according as it appeareth unto you by Equity, joining together that which is assembled to its natural figure."
"But if the fracture is in the long or that which hath declined to the interior parts thereof, then thou shalt make the patient lie upon his belly within such preparation as thou hast for the restoration of that fracture." "And then when you have joined it, lay upon it a plaster, and apply also upon that asthma of wood or bone, and strengthen it by straitness so that thou needest not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint."
"And join also the concavity with that which filleth it within, and then let him take the straitness according to equality; and command the patient to sleep upon his back or upon his side."
"But if thou shalt perceive an aposteme hard or hot, then cease from the extension and restoration thereof until such time as that aposteme be assuaged, and lay upon it that which assuageth it according as is before spoken; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it even as it behoveth."
"And if thou shalt perceive such in the bone set or comminuted, then thou shall make them whole."
"Chapter xi"
"Of the restoration of a fracture of the thumb."
"The thumb is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder."
"And if it be broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two manners: and the first manner is this, thou shalt take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness of this fashion:"
There are two bands at both extremes, and it should be suspended from a raised place.
The patient should lie on a chair, and extend their broken arm over the backrest so that it reaches the tip of the backrest where the curve of the backrest is attached.
And suspend some heavy weight over that, or the servant stretches it out towards its parts; and then the doctor places his hands on the fracture with his fingers together, so that the fracture returns according to what is necessary.
Another method is for the patient to be propped up on their back and their arm suspended from a sling around their neck. Then, two servants are commanded: one holds what is above the fracture with both hands, while the other holds what is below it; each extends their part accordingly.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie another bandage over the fracture and also tie one onto that, and each of the two servants extends their part accordingly.
And if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then one half of the bandage should be placed on the tip and the other below the fracture towards the elbow.
"If a fracture extends to the hip joint, then it is necessary to place a bandage over that area and around the hip."
"If it heals easily and without difficulty, and especially when it is healing properly with good application, then stretch it a little if there is no inflammation."
"However, if inflammation occurs, then leave the bandage in place for about 7 days and change it frequently with warm water and rose oil compresses until the inflammation subsides."
"The method of stretching it is to apply a plaster cast over the fracture and also place a napkin wrap over the plaster cast. Then adjust the arm with that device and place your hand gently on their muscle."
"And pause the napkin and stretch over the arm support device so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless prohibited due to some cause by the broken bone."
"Then use splints."
"And it is necessary to apply that splint which is placed on the inside and fits tightly with the others; place between each splint a pad of suitable width and make the length of the splints according to the fracture, adding three fingers' breadth beyond either side of the break."
"And also stretch over the splints with the tension which has been mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"And it is necessary that your tension be stronger at the site of the fracture." "The longer the fracture, the less tension should be applied."
"Therefore, if you find yourself in the situation of having placed the splints and tension as we have said when restoring the member to its normal position, then do it."
"And if you suspect inflammation is developing, then leave the tension and splints on for about 7 days as we have said."
"And also consider the limb in all cases for three days so that no pressure or inflammation occurs; or if food cannot reach the member due to excess tension, then correct everything as we have said."
"But if you are uncertain whether this might cause some harm, do not remove the bandage for at least three days or more."
"And let his bed be inclined upwards at the head so that he may sleep comfortably and have his hands on his stomach."
"And place over the cooking support a full pot of wool wadding and consider every hour of the night or day that the figure of the broken limb is not displaced nor dissolved, or if the limb is softened, then correct everything with your skill."
"And place food for the patient according to the order which we have mentioned earlier, ensuring that the food is finely chopped at first so that it can be swallowed easily; and then gradually make the food coarser."
"However, it is customary for the splint and bandage to be left on for 40 days, after which they should be removed and a warm bath administered along with medicines that will correct any remaining issues."
"And if the fracture is still unhealed, do not remove the limb and splints for up to 10 days or even up to two months."
"Chapter XII."
"On the reduction of fractures of the arm."
"The arm consists of two bones called the 'two forks'; one is small and it is this that supports the thumb, while the other is large and is situated below the small finger."
"And sometimes only the larger fork may be fractured, or just the smaller one, but it also happens that they are both broken together."
"And when the small fork is fractured above, its reduction will be easier and its healing will also be quicker."
"However, when the fork of the arm below the fracture is broken, its reduction is difficult and its healing is slow."
"And it is even worse when both bones are fractured together."
"Therefore, if the bone that is fractured is the small fork above, it is appropriate for the physician to perform its reduction by gently pulling on it until it becomes straight."
"But if it is the large fork that is broken, then more forceful traction must be applied."
"And if both forks are broken together, then you must apply traction with even greater force."
"And during the reduction and traction of the arm, it is necessary for the hand to be placed on a pillow with the palm facing upwards, and for the thumb to be kept above all the other fingers and for the patient to be centered on a well-cushioned chair."
"And place a pillow in front of him at the level of his elbow or shoulder, so that he does not exert himself."
"Then one assistant should extend the arm from below with their hand or a bandage, while another assistant extends it from above in the same manner."
"And then the physician should align the bone so that it can be reduced to its best possible position."
"If during the reduction of the bone any resistance is felt, then you must use all your skill to overcome it and reduce it completely into its place."
"And if during reduction it appears that the elbow joint is dislocated and this was not expected, then you must reduce it according to the method which we have described above."
"But if the fracture is accompanied by inflammation, I have already spoken of this in another chapter, so take care to treat it accordingly."
"And if an abscess forms at the beginning of your reduction, then take a cloth soaked in cerot made from rose oil and white wax, which should be neither too thick nor too thin, and apply it with gentle pressure until the abscess subsides. Then remove the cerot and apply a plaster made from powdered mallow with clay of egg whites, along with splints; and ensure that the splint placed over the fracture is broad and strong."
"And know that in the case of fractures of the arm, it usually requires from two to four splints, depending on whether there is a fracture of one fork or both forks together."
"And then apply your bandage over the site of the fracture with great force and firmness; but if the traction is towards the upper part of the fracture or below it, then apply the bandage a little more loosely according to what was said at the beginning of this chapter."
"And the bandages wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough; and the plaster with which it is strengthened should be neither too thick nor too thin, as we have recommended."
"And consider the limb and splint after several days." "And if any cause arises which requires adjustment, such as swelling of the limb, then you must immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling ceases."
"Then allow the limb to rest without constraint until it has regained its strength, and then reapply the bandage."
"And if the traction is softened and the bone has returned to its proper position, then correct everything with your skill."
"And also consider whether the limb is being deprived of nutrition due to excessive tightness of the bandage; if so, then loosen it a little and leave it like that for several days until nourishment can reach the limb again, and then reapply it."
"But if the patient does not experience any of the causes that we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandage for at least twenty days or so."
"And then hang the patient's hand from its own strength of movement, and have them lie on their back to sleep."
"And know that this fracture is usually restored in thirty days or thirty-two days, but it may be restored even more quickly depending on the disposition of the complexion and the strength of the individual."
"Chapter XIII."
"On the restoration of fractures of the little finger and those mentioned above."
"You should know that in the little finger and the toes of the said hand, fracture often occurs, but this rarely happens except for contusions many times."
"And when fracture or contusion occurs in the hand, it is necessary for the patient to lie down and rest on their back with their arms at equal height, placing the affected hand gently above the other extended one."
"And then the servant extends the broken bones and the doctor puts them back together so that they fit well with good alignment."
"And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and gauze, unless there is a wound that needs cauterization. Afterwards, the splint should be applied on top according to the size of the location, and you already have that wrapped in linen cloth."
"But if the fracture is on the inner side of the palm near the belly of the hand, then make a similar bandage out of cloth and command the patient to hold that with their palm wrapped tightly; afterwards it should be wrapped with a long cloth."
"And let the splint be made of leather covered in cloth so that the leather adheres to the palm. And wrap it according to what is necessary."
"But if the fracture is on the outer side of the palm, then it is necessary to apply splints above and below the metacarpal bones on the palm so that the hand is elevated and straight. And afterwards bind with a bandage, as if wrapping around a nut between these said ligaments."
"And if there is a fracture in one of the bones of the said fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be aligned as necessary and then wrapped around the palm. And if you wish, apply a small splint on top to elevate the fracture and prevent movement."
"But if the fracture is in one of these fingers such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger or little finger, then align it and wrap it with its respective finger. Or they can all be wrapped together continuously, which is better. Or apply a small splint on top, as we have said for the thumb."
"And consider, in your treatment, and especially that one, the position of an abscess if it is hot and opposed — with your operation — to what should be done when such a cause occurs according to what has been said about this."
"Chapter XIV"
"On the treatment of fractures of the hipbone."
"The hip bone of the thigh is often broken, its condition declared by feeling according to its transformation at the groin and the pubic region."
"And its restoration is accomplished in such a way that the ligaments are stretched over the fracture and other ligaments leave the fracture, and the patient lies prostrate on their stomach and face."
"And accordingly, the minister extends all the ligament to its part according to its quality when the fracture is in the middle of the bone."
"But if it is displaced at the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be brought close to the back at the part of the knee, so that the extension goes downwards, and the other ligament leaves the fracture."
"And similarly, if the break is displaced at the back, then the ligament should be brought close to the back and the extension should go to the parts that are left."
"And accordingly the doctor tightens the bone on both its sides until it reduces according to its natural shape and the bone is joined by good union. At this point it is necessary to apply plaster and strapping, so that there may not occur gangrene in the limb." "But if gangrene occurs, then you should leave it for several days until the gangrene is completely healed and then return to your treatment."
"The strapping should be done in this way: you should wrap a firm bandage over the fracture and fold it two or three times, removing any excess. Then you should wrap the leg so that it reaches the ankle and root of the thigh."
"And you should insert a long needle between the thigh and the leg near the knee joint on both sides. Then bring the ends of the thread out from above each of the two parts, and then wind them around the leg and thigh to remove any excess bandage."
"And then you should tie it over the thigh at the site of that fracture once or twice, and tie one knot on the bone of the leg."
"And then fill in any spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the strapping is firm."
"And then you should begin with a strong strapping of the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four wraps. And whenever you tighten the ligament at the site of the fracture, your strapping should be looser and lighter and softer."
"And then extend both ends of the thread to the ankle joint on both sides, and tighten them with knots on the other side so that the ligament does not come loose from its place."
"And then leave the strapping on until there is no occurrence of corrosion of the limb, gangrene, cauterization, inflammation or resemblance to those. And if any such condition occurs, then you should remove it immediately and correct what has happened at once, as we have said many times."
"But if the bone sticks out where it has been broken, then you should reduce it as much as possible. And if not, then make a cut over it and remove that, and treat it with what is customary for such cases until it heals."
"And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with stitches tied around it as we have said in the case of the adjutator and the arm."
"But however you restore it in this way, do not let the patient limp. And if it is restored alone, without applying the other leg, then it will be necessary for him to limp."
"And know that the thigh is strengthened in forty days or a few months, depending on the diversity of constitutions and other conditions."
"Chapter XV."
"On the restoration of a fracture of the kneecap."
"You should know that a fracture of the patella rarely happens on its own, but often occurs along with bruising."
"But if it does happen, then it is only a crack or split in its parts, and this is with pain or without pain. And that is all about the sensation."
"To restore him, it is necessary that what has been broken in his limbs be bound with the said entrails and joined together as much as possible for equality and ease of construction."
"Then apply plaster and compress over that round stone if needed. Then bind it with sufficient strength according to what is appropriate."
"And consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in other fractures, thus as if it were an apostem caut and similar to those so that it opposes all accidents with what is convenient for him until he is healed."
"Chapter XVI"
"Of the restoration of a fracture of the thighbone."
"Know that in the thighbone there are two bones of which one is thick and is called by the name 'thighbone'; and the other is thin, and is called 'focil'."
"And it happens to some of the kinds of fracture that happen to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is made like the restoration of the arm simultaneously, and its operation is one."
"And if both bones are broken together, the thighbone becomes completely shattered."
"If only the thin bone is broken, the thighbone becomes partially broken anteriorly."
"And if only the thick bone is broken, it remains behind that one, and it belongs to those causes which are not simultaneous ones."
"And therefore administer extension and equatio and ligament of the splinters according to this equality."
"But nevertheless it is necessary, if the fracture of the thighbone is high having many attempts, that extension be lesser and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue."
"And in the thighbone there is some cause of operation over the arm, and it is when you align the splinters and complete all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood from pine which is placed behind the causes which are placed below, and they are made similarly to branches of palm; select those that are less thick and not too many, and their length should be according to the length of the thighbone, and from the navel to the middle."
"And therefore wrap it around each rope in two wrappings according to its length, and place one on the fracture of the thighbone on one side and the other on the other side, and they should be from the navel to about a third below the knee."
"And therefore bind the two splinters in three places on both extremities and at the middle one, for with this strapping the thighbone is prevented from bending to the right or left side, and stands in good position."
"And now a splint of wood according to the length of the thighbone has been administered; and it is placed on it so that it retains its movement."
"Especially it is necessary that you do this in fracture, especially when with it there is abundant bleeding."
"And therefore examine the thighbone every day and study with it for final study, and you guard against apostema and inflammation or other similar causes; and whenever some cause of those occurs, then place it on it again with what is necessary until it is healed."
"Chapter XVII."
"Of the fracture of the ankle bone and of the bones of the foot and of those ones."
"In the heel, there is no fracture ever; but in the bones of the foot, fractures happen sometimes; and in those of the foot, scarcely ever, and only attrition occurs during periods of disposition."
"If the bones of the foot happen to be fractured, sometimes those bones become elevated above themselves among them, then the patient places their foot on the ground prepared thus while walking."
"And properly pauses the foot over that which is elevated by those bones, and presses them down, grinds them, and pinches them around where they return."
"And properly applies plaster and stops on top, and places under the sole of the foot a small table with two flat heads."
"And properly stretch that ankle with downward traction, since you have wrapped it with cloths and other causes of which you stand in need."
"And after three or four days have passed, then you untie the bandage, for you will find the bones squeezed together, either pinched or displaced, and the operation in those is thus known."
"But if some are pinched together, then you restore them and squeeze them thus with oil and join them together as you would in the restoration of those fingers." "And properly applies splint to the pinched finger according to its length, and places it thus with some slant; and properly places under the foot this table which has been recommended to you, and stretches that ankle with downward traction."
"But if what is pinched among the bones are two or three or more, then you apply splints to each finger according to its quantity wrapped in lion's skin, and stretches the table under the sole of the foot, with one of the heads of the table facing up towards the sole of the foot so that it may have good retention."
"And remember, do not neglect that you should not consider all the causes which have been mentioned to you in the other fractures of accidents that we have said; and apply treatment according to each accident with what is appropriate."
"Chapter XVIII."
"Of the fracture of the hip bone in women and of the bone of the pubis and the penis in men."
"When the hip bone of a woman is pinched together, then cut that with this saw, and properly incline it at the junction of both sides slightly towards the back."
"And properly the midwife pushes the hip bone together and makes it so that it may fit together like a sphere; and properly the woman is turned upside down, and gradually lifts up both sides of her until all is forgotten before the hip bone comes to fit together like a sphere with the bones pinched together."
"And properly applies powders on the hip bone, and when she wants to urinate, gently moves the hip bone with ease and then she urinates, and reduces it back again in the manner by which it was moved at the beginning, and then returns to her first powder."
"And do this for seven days or about those days, because that is restored."
"And if you wish, take an ovine bladder and stretch over the hip bone a reed cannula; and introduce all of the bladder into the hip bone, and then insufflate through the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated within the hip bone because the fracture returns."
"And properly she is turned upright and stays like that for some days as we have recommended, until it is healed."
"And when the bone of the pubis in a man or woman is pinched together, then administers in its restoration and fixation that which we have recommended for the hip bone."
"And that which is right is not hidden in these fractures close together and which have little expense, especially for those who study much and use and understand this my book; because that fracture signifies to you the greatest positions straight up in the restoration and tightening of it, and therefore understand well."
"But in the penis of a man when it is pinched together, take a gourd of oil and put the penis into that; and properly applies around it wrapping with cloth, and then leaves it for three days or about those days, and it will be healed."
"Chapter XIX."
"Of the restoration of fractures of bones when they are with pus."
"It is convenient for that one to whom it happens a fracture with pus, especially if the bone is large such as the thighbone or humerus, or similar to that one of which you are careful and perform phlebotomy at once if the conditions for phlebotomy to you or testify like this as we have said above."
"And if the pus flows out bloody, then it is necessary for you to retain that in such a way that you powder over it with dry plaster, unless another cause is present."
"And properly begins restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong it if otherwise an abscess does not occur."
"But if an abscess occurs hard, then you leave the restoration of it for nine days until the abscess is softened, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day of all, because you will cause bad accidents."
"But if the broken bone protrudes over the uncovered elbow joint, then it is necessary for you to perforate that and reduce it and align it with your hands gently and with little extension."
"But if extension is not prepared for you and the equating of it with your hands, then reduce that with your hands with this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, the length of which is seven digits or eight and its width according to the quantity of pus. And therefore it is necessary for the physician to take from those three or four according to the quantity that is needed for that in the cure in all kinds of fracture."
"And let it be round and let there be little roughness on it so that it is not doubled by the pressure over it at the time of the operation."
"And let it have an acute extremity with which to insert into its extremities; and let the top of it be thicker tending, and of its middle part thin."
"And this is the shape of it:"
"In Greek it is named 'la brake ventez atelarem', which means little shovel."
"Why should the extremity of the leg be elevated above the prominence of the bone?"
"And press it in such a way that the bone returns to its place, and remains stable with some device."
"And then try to align the extremities of the fracture between them. If one extremity is thin and does not take well with the tool for good reason, you should insert the extremity of that bone so that the tool has power over it."
"But if you cannot reduce the bone with what has been recommended, then use whatever tool you deem fit among those mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you."
"And finally polish that which remains on the bone of its roughness and small scratches."
And when the bone is reduced and yet the reduction causes the patient to feel violent pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; therefore do this that you may greatly benefit the patient.
Once you have completed your reduction of the bone and then immerse a piece of linen in black wine vinegar, especially if it is summer, do not allow any grease or other ointment to remain on the surface, so that no foulness or corruption may occur.
And precisely at the time of completing your reduction of the bone, administer plaster bandages and leave the surface uncovered in such a way that you may insert them folded and use wrappings according to the quantity of the bandage.
And be very careful that you do not stretch the plaster bandage over the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this and caused harm to their patients by producing mortification or gangrene. And if your reduction is light, soft, and different from the usual reduction of other fractures.
And if the bandage is too large or of poor quality, and you fear that it might cause one of those harmful accidents which we have mentioned, and there is pain in the place, then it would not be appropriate to use plaster bandages. Instead, use a dressing of hard linen cloth and stretch it over with great care.
And after about two days, when the bandage has become dry and hard, remove it carefully with vinegar. Then apply compresses to keep warm, using those of our usual custom for treating plaster bandages, such as tetrafarmacon ointment or similar ones.
You should also remove the lining and examine the bandage every morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm. Make sure that the limb is positioned in such a way that the pus can flow easily from the wound to its outer parts.
If many days have passed and the plaster bandage is not consolidated nor separated from the limb, then you should know that this is due to some small errors. Therefore, you should examine the bandage with care. And of those errors, those that are separated and removed should be replaced, while those that do not separate but compress the limb and cause pain should be incised according to what is possible for you to do.
And if the bandage becomes warm or inflamed, this is a sign of inflammation or some other kind of corruption and putrefaction. Therefore, you should pause here for one of those which are mentioned in their own chapter regarding its treatment.
And as for this matter where I should speak more plainly and you present your opinion: when the bone is crushed, do not attempt to expose it like the bones of the wrist or elbow or similar ones of the large limbs. Do not try to attract it nor extract it because many times death has occurred due to this. Leave that alone until it has fallen out by itself in about twenty to thirty days. Then treat the wound if you can see it, otherwise leave it be.
Chapter XX.
Of the treatment of node formation that occurs at the site of some fracture.
Very often node formation occurs at the site of fracture healing, especially near joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb, preventing its natural movement.
Therefore, if the node is recent, apply to it medicines that have astringent properties such as Aloes, Rue, and Myrrh. Also consider using Serracollo or Acacia and similar ones. Take some of these and mix them with wine that has been astringent, or with albumen of egg white, or with vinegar, and apply this mixture to the node using linen cloth, securing it with a good dressing.
"Leave the bandage on for several days without removing it; then remove it and apply another until the swelling has gone down; or press this sheet of lead descended onto it, for lead has the property to reduce everything that is swollen in the members."
"If the swelling has already turned into bone and needs to be cut open, then carefully cut it along the upper part of the wound, incise the excess flesh and membrane, then rinse it with warm water until it is healed."
"Chapter XX."
"The restoration of a fracture when it has been restored, and if the limb remains subtle and does not grow according to its nature."
"When a bone fracture is restored, and if the limb remains subtle and frail, this usually happens for several reasons: one of which is an excess of solution of the ligaments and their formation not according to what is appropriate; or due to excess scarring of the ligaments preventing proper nutrition from reaching the limb; or due to excessive broaching of joints; or due to excessive movement not at its proper time; or due to weakness of blood in the body and debility."
"The treatment for this is to give the patient food so that their body becomes robust and blood is multiplied within them; administer baths; and apply warmth and joy upon them."
"And if there is still weakness in the limb, apply a plaster above it so that the plaster draws out excess moisture, or continue to massage it with warm water until nutrition returns to its natural state."
"Chapter XXII"
"Regarding the treatment of crushed bones that are restored crookedly and require surgical correction."
"When it happens that a limb has healed with crookedness and prominence at the crushed bone site, causing deformity of the limb but not preventing its natural operation, then do not listen to those who claim that the limb is newly crushed."
"But many foolish doctors and healers have done this in our land, and this operation is very blameworthy and leads to great fear whose sign is death."
"But if lameness and stiffness are recent, then they should be massaged with warm water containing softening herbs such as marshmallow and rose hips, mallows, and similar ones; and a plaster made from mallows should be applied; or a poultice made from rose hip mucilage and kneaded with dough or oil of cirami should be applied; or figs fried in pigeon droppings and ground together should be applied as those medicines known as consolidators."
"And sometimes with continuous light friction it can be dissolved even in the most humid parts, and movement of the limb should be administered in all directions at all times."
"But if the crookedness has already advanced and is hard and converted into bone and surgery with iron is necessary, then it is appropriate to cut off what is above the break and dissolve the continuity of the bone, and carefully remove excess callus from that place using fine scissors, and facilitate mobility in that area through diligent and careful study."
"Chapter XXIII"
Of speech in dislocation
Dislocation is the displacement of some of the joints in their location, preventing them from moving, distorting the shape of the limb, and causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore when dislocation occurs, it should be reduced as soon as possible, and not delayed, because if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and it is difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And therefore at that time one should not be moved or stretched out, because many times it causes spasm and severe pain to the patient.
But when this happens, then one should resort to the phlebotomy of the patient, and let him rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be immersed in hot water and oil, and thus reduced with ease.
And every joint should be treated with that which brings it back to its original position.
And hereafter I will explain the chapters on dislocation as they are presented in fracture, from the head downwards to the foot.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
When both jaws are dislocated at once, they are reduced by little movement and mollified a bit; or sometimes they are completely dislocated, until they can be mollified at the point of articulation, when saliva begins to flow from the patient's mouth, indicating that he cannot open his mouth nor close it, and his tongue is stuck in his throat.
But when the dislocation of the jaw is slight, in many cases it returns to its place by itself without any help.
But if the dislocation is complete and entire, then reduction should be administered gently, and not delayed at all.
And let the attendant hold the patient's head, and insert his finger into the joint of the dislocation of the jaw inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the others should press from outside with which to balance. And tell the patient to keep his jaws still and not move them from their position; then gently push the jaw back into place until it returns to its proper position.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated at once, then evaporation with hot water and oil should be administered until easy reduction can be made without delay.
And when they return to their place and are well engaged, and the mouth of the patient is closed but not mollified, then apply poultices of cloth with cerot, and make them of wax and rose oil; and let them be tied on easily with soft linen.
And let the patient sleep on his back, with his head between two pillows, so that he cannot move it to the right or left; and do not force him to mastication at all until the pain is gone and the jaws are well engaged. Then he may chew what he wishes.
And when this is administered with ease, and do not work upon the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, or occlusion until the jaw is engaged and healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated and not returning to their places at once, then often hot applications may be used with soda, and sometimes a gentle purgative is administered to relieve the belly of the patient, and sometimes pure calomel.
And when you do this, know that it is harmful; for many times the person to whom this happens dies in a few days.
Chapter XXV
Of the reduction of dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from its place by the side due to its continuity with the sternum; but sometimes it is dislocated on the outside and this is detected by touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms. Then press the dislocation with your palm with a strong pressure, for it will return to its place.
And then apply plasters and poultices and strengthen that side.
But the extremity of that which seizes the heart and is continuous with it is not dislocated at any time. Therefore, it must be reduced and engaged as has been said, according to your ability.
And then apply plasters, poultices, and bandages, and tell the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI
"On the reduction of a dislocation of the muscle."
"You should know that the muscle cannot be dislocated except in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated at the beginning of the little finger."
"And the second way is that it is dislocated at the top of the muscle, which happens rarely. It does not dislocate at the joints behind the shoulder or in front by the nerves."
"And especially it is dislocated and pushed out at the joints of the little finger near the thumb, and this happens more easily in those with less flesh, whereas in those with much flesh, it comes out with difficulty."
"And sometimes it happens to some men that the muscle swells due to a blow or contusion, and an abscess forms which bursts open, causing the muscle to become dislocated."
"Therefore you should proceed with caution in treating such a case, and then proceed with its cure."
"And the dislocation of it is known when it occurs at the joints of the little finger near the thumb; so that you may distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the sound one, because there is great diversity among them. And you find a concavity in the head of the muscle on the side of the little finger near the touch of the head of the muscle like a hole, and the sick person cannot bring his hand to his ear nor move it with any movement."
"If it is dislocated towards the foot or above, you will then find a clear and visible touch, not hidden."
"And this dislocation can be easily reduced when it is fresh and the patient is young."
"And the reduction of it is that the physician raises the hand of the patient above its joints; and approaches with his fingers on either side of the little finger of the patient, and raises the joint with strength to its proper place, while the physician raises the hand of the patient, and extends it upwards; and then approaches it again for it returns easily."
"But if it does not return by what we have said, and some days have passed since the dislocation occurred, then the patient should be bathed in warm water, and administered an embrocation that softens and lubricates like if it were a poultice made of althea root, fenugreek, and marshmallow in water."
"And then the patient should be propped up on his back and paused with his fingers on either side of the little finger until it is loosened between elevation and tension, and then the physician places his heel on the sphere and raises the head of the muscle with strength, and draws the hand of the patient so that it is not hidden at its joints, and another attendant holds the head of the patient so that it is not hidden at its joints for it soon returns to its proper place."
"And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: And have present a man who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on that side; and insert his muscle between the little finger of the patient and raise the little finger of the patient to its joints while the patient is suspended in air, and another attendant draws the hand of the patient from its joints towards its belly." "But if the patient is thin, then another cause should be suspended for him to lean on, lest he cause harm, for the dislocation returns quickly."
"And it is reduced in the same way according to another manner: And have placed on the ground a staff whose head is round in shape like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin; and let the patient lean on its side; then let him place his hands on either side of the other end of the staff; then let him extend his hand on the other side; and let him extend his body from that side with strength for the joint returns to its proper place with speed."
"And if the reduction of it is difficult by all the causes that we have mentioned, then use this cure: And take a staff whose length is two cubits and whose width is four digits; and let its thickness be lessened at the end of the little finger in this shape:"
"And place lions' skin on the round head of it for fear that the staff should slip from the patient."
"And let it be placed on either side of the little finger of the patient; and let his hand or arm be extended over the staff to its joints; and let the staff be tied on the assistant's back, on the arm and on the end of the hand."
"And let him place his arm on a step of a ladder according to its width, and let his hand be extended at its joints; and let the other body be suspended from the other side for the joint enters quickly."
"And when the entrance of it is completed according to each manner that is possible for you; then place a bandage of wool of equal quantity on either side of the little finger; and then place an emplaster made from powder of millstone with frankincense and album of egg over all the muscle, thus as it is turned upwards; and then bind it tightly with tension decently; and let the bandage be turned around the emplaster; and let his hand be joined to its collar; and let him be left; and let not his hand be moved for seven days."
"And it is necessary that the diet of the patient be lightened until the member is comforted, for it is easier for it to be healed."
"And then let him be given abundant food after seven or eight days when he has exercised a movement; and if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed."
"But if the joint is dislocated many times by humidity that occurs to it or by another cause, then cauterization should be administered to it with three strokes according to what is said before in the chapter on cauterization."
"And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm; and the emplaster and the binding are reduced upon it many times; and it is not firm or hard; and he cannot raise his arm to its joints, then know that the nerves of it, which are at the head of the muscle, are either cut off or stretched or softened; and know that the joint will never be placed in its proper place."
"For the dislocation of that which is made in the knee and breast or joints behind, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands until it returns."
"And let another binding be administered to it; and cure it until it is healed."
"And if heaviness occurs in the member during healing and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is lessened and it returns to its former nature."
"Chapter xxvii."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder."
"Know that the joint of the shoulder is dislocated with difficulty and returns likewise; and it is dislocated at all parts, but especially at the front part behind, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident by looking or touching according to whatever figure it may be dislocated. And when you adjust the dislocated shoulder blade with the palm of your hand, that is made manifestly clear to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor touch itself with its muscle."
"And you should choose it and reduce the dislocation as soon as the hot swelling comes because if the hot swelling comes to it, the reduction becomes difficult; and even then it is not completely healed. And especially when it is at the back parts because there it is worse than all other kinds of dislocations and causes more pain, and many deaths occur with that."
The restoration of the patient, when it is possible to reduce him, is that his hand be extended with two masses and his arm be expanded, and the physician's hands be on his knee and let him press the joint with the fists of both his hands together or with the back of his palm.
However if the dislocation is at the palms in front, then he should return as follows: by bending his hand on his own hand, so that he strikes his knee with the back of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then apply strong traction and powerful force.
And two assistants should extend the arm, holding it firmly with two assistants so that it is not moved during traction.
And furthermore the arm should be rotated in every direction, because it is wrapped around with doubled cloth or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with some oil to facilitate lubrication of the joint; and moreover he should press the joint vigorously until it returns.
And also the reduction of the elbow that is paused on that empastre in which there is stipitation and excretion with albumen of a bird's egg, and let it be extracted with gentle compression, and the arm should be suspended from the neck for several days.
And also if it is dislocated again, then untie it; and afterwards if the joint has been formed in its proper place, then untie the bandage from it and leave it alone.
And if the joint does not become stronger, then repeat the emplaster and bandage, leaving it for several days until it is healed; and moreover untie that.
But if during the reduction of the joint there occurs gravity in that part, and the disposition is in motion, then apply humidification in a bath with friction and compression until it is alleviated; or place on the joint an alleviation plaster so that it may be of great benefit; and moreover bind it up, leaving it over the bone day and night. And afterwards take off the plaster, and put him in a bath; and when he gets up, then rub the joint with gentle friction, and moreover repeat over it an alleviating plaster so that it may be of great benefit, once, twice, three times And meanwhile keep him in bed until he is cured.
And if you wish, place on that a wet cowhide fold and stick it on him; and do this several times, because it is moist and reduces inflammation.
Chapter XXVIII
Of the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder blade, which is the joint between the arm and the hand
When the shoulder blade of the arm is dislocated occasionally, its reduction is easy; and on the contrary with other joints, but there should be speed in reducing it at the very moment when it has been dislocated before an apostema occurs or that some accident happens in the apostema caut.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and make him extend his arm; and then the doctor places his hand or his palm over the prominence of the joint and pushes it gently until it returns to its proper place.
But it should be taken into consideration whether the palm is already turned towards the front of the hand; if it is, then let the patient place the back of his hand on a table for extension and reduction; but if the dislocation was behind the hand, then let the position of his hand be intrinsically upon the table so that the hand of the doctor may fall upon that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns soon, that is good. But if not, then strengthen it with an emplaster which allows the apostema to subside and leave him; and do not repeat this, because otherwise it would be impossible for you to effect its reduction afterwards when several days have passed.
But if the joint remains tortuous then do not inform the patient of any cause, but tell him that his arm is softened and cannot be strengthened because of some cause. Then you should know that the nerves are pressed or cut, and therefore it does not improve unless it is strengthened by cautery near its attachment and this procedure is followed several times with some cause.
Therefore when the dislocation has returned, then place on it the plaster which we have recommended and let him be left at rest for three days; and moreover bind it up and let him be left at rest with exercises of his arm.
But if the motion of it is stopped and some gravity occurs thereupon, then treat it with warm water and gentle friction several times until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX
Of the treatment of dislocation of those [joints].
Sometimes these joints become dislocated all at once.
And when any of these fingers is dislocated on the back or palm side of the hand, then stretch that finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs until it returns.
And afterwards support the head of the finger and suspend it from the part where it was dislocated, leaving it thus for two days.
And later suspend that [finger] again and stretch it until it is completely straightened within that same day.
And when night falls, bind that [finger] according to this description, and do not cease loosening it during the day and exercising it with movements, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it is straight.
And do likewise if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it at that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
Similarly, do this in all other cases of dislocation.
**Chapter 30.**
Of the treatment of dislocation of the spinal joints.
When dislocation of one or more spinal joints occurs in the back or neck, and many vertebrae are displaced from their proper places, then there is no hope for cure at that time because death usually ensues.
And the sign of this is that evacuation of the bowels occurs involuntarily and cannot be retained. Many times some limbs become paralyzed on one side or both sides, either one leg or both arms, or one arm. Sometimes one vertebra is displaced from its proper place because many times displacement occurs in four directions.
Therefore, it has already been displaced to the back and is called scoliosis (curvature of the spine).
But its treatment is as follows: first consider if scoliosis occurred in childhood because then there is no cure and it cannot be completely healed.
But those who come later with treatment by plaster or percussion or other similar methods have already discussed the ways of treatment in lengthy speeches. However, I will briefly discuss it as follows because it is appropriate to my intended purpose and I have already explained and demonstrated many instruments for this purpose.
And only those can be cured whose scoliosis occurs specifically in the posterior part as I will recount later.
And let the patient lie on their face with their head close to a wall. Let them be covered with soft pillows so that the spine does not get injured.
And place a straight stick on the ground near the head of the patient and another stick under their feet at the other end.
And let one assistant hold the stick near the head but not in a pit, and another assistant hold the other according to that form.
And place a cap on the patient's head and tie it firmly with soft cloth. Extend the end of one bandage to the stick placed above the patient's head, attach it there, and extend another bandage over the hips and knees of the patient towards both sides. Then adjust all the bandages and attach them to the other stick placed near the patient's feet.
And then each assistant extends the bandages to their respective sticks. The two sticks and the bandages should not be removed from their places but should remain there without being too tightly fastened as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns to its proper position. Or he may place one foot on that part and adjust himself there. Then he places his feet on the table so that he can return.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a table whose width or length is about three braccia. Then make a hole in the wall near the patient where the end of the table can fit into it. And place the middle of the table on the spine. The doctor places his feet on the other end and presses firmly until the spine is corrected and returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, use a wrench to turn it back with your hand. Lie on the ground near the patient's head between two sticks whose length is one braccio each and place another stick of the same length under their head in the same way. Make sure that there is a palm's width between each stick and make a groove in each stick for the cord to pass through. Ensure that each stick is firmly fixed on the ground so that it does not move.
And place a round stick in which the wrench is inserted into both grooves of the two sticks.
And on one end of it there is a hole in which the stick is formed. The length of this stick is one palm with a groove for turning. The other two sticks have similar holes.
And then tighten the bandages that you have stretched over the spine of the patient on the stick near their head. And also tighten the bandages that you have stretched over their hips and knees onto the stick near their feet.
And then on each stick place an assistant who will turn the wrench with their hand while the doctor presses the spine back into its proper position as we have said.
And once the spine has been corrected and returned to its proper position, then apply a plaster consisting of chalk and album ground together with egg whites. Then place gauze on it and place the plaster on a table whose width is about three digits or somewhat more. The length should be equal to the size of the swelling and placed over the affected part of the spine.
And then wrap it with bandages as we have said and feed the patient a subtle diet until they are healed.
And if there is still some swelling at the end of the healing process, then apply medicines that soften and cleanse using the application of the table for a long time. We have already recommended this method, and it should be applied to that area with a lead plate.
And sometimes swelling occurs at the back of the lower spine due to that dislocation. That bone already threatens death and therefore no pressure should be applied to it with this medicine because it will cause great harm.
Chapter XXI
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip joint.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the thigh meet only by dislocation alone. This does not occur at other joints with small movements or concavities.
And the hip joint can become dislocated in four ways: either inward or outward at its front parts or backward at its rear parts.
It is most commonly dislocated inwardly, and less frequently anteriorly or posteriorly.
The sign of hip dislocation inwardly is that when you compare the length of the healthy limb with the sick one, the sick one appears shorter and its thighbone sticks out more prominently than the healthy one. The patient cannot bend their leg or knee inward, and this area becomes inflamed as if there were a hot abscess. This indicates that the head of the femur has moved into this position.
The sign of dislocation occurring at its outer parts is that its symptoms are opposite to those mentioned previously.
"The sign of one who has suffered a dislocation in front is when the chamber expands according to its nature, yet that part does not bend without pain; if one tries to walk, they cannot do so without discomfort. The urine is retained and the swelling is inflamed, and movement causes pain in the heel."
"The sign of one who has suffered a dislocation in the back is that the knee does not straighten and cannot bend without pain; the chamber of it is shorter than the other one, the swelling is softened, and the head of the matter is turned towards that place."
"But reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you should consider whether the dislocation is ancient and old, which has already become accustomed to having that approach. You then administer reduction according to its condition, but it does not enter into total cure; therefore no traction should be made in that case."
