"But in one who has recently suffered a dislocation of any of the four species, you should quickly turn the joint back into place and extend it on both front and rear parts. Then move it to the right and left until it returns completely; then no other treatment is necessary."
"But if it does not return in this manner, then you should prepare a strong bandage and extend the chamber of it from the farthest part, or with its own muscles, or by attaching a bandage above the bone. Then another assistant should extend the arm of that person upwards so that their hands are placed behind their buttocks. And again stretch tightly with a bandage on the root of the matter, and have another assistant hold the extremity of the bandage."
"And stretch it either from its front part to its angle at the collarbone or from its rear part to its angle at the shoulder blade."
"And all these stretches should be done in one session while the patient is lying on their back with their buttocks elevated and knees hanging down."
"This species of extension applies generally to all four species [of dislocation]."
"And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be well. But if not, then it is necessary for each species to undergo its own specific treatment."
"For reduction of its own kind, when it is dislocated from the front parts, the patient should be made to sit up on their left side. Then bind the bandages tightly over the root of the matter at that point between the head of the bone and the angle of the collarbone. And then extend the bandage upwards from the angle of the collarbone towards the upper part of the shoulder blade."
"And then another strong assistant should take hold of both of his arms and press the thick part of the bone against it while stretching forcefully outward so that it returns to its proper place."
"And this species is that which requires the easiest reduction by which this member is returned, compared to the other species [of dislocation]."
"But if it is not reduced by this species of treatment and does not obey the return, then you should lift both legs of the patient together with a light, loose bandage over both thighs and buttocks."
"And let the length of each be as much as four fingerbreadths, and let the bandage be extended further than the other by two fingerbreadths."
"And then suspend the patient over the head of the bed, with their feet on the floor and their legs extended. The distance from the ground should be about two spans."
"And then command a strong youth to press down on the head of the bone and suspend himself together with the patient. And another youth should forcefully press down on the bone so that the joint returns quickly to its proper place."
"But in the proper reduction when it is dislocated outward, the patient should lie on their back as we have recommended in cases of dislocation with swelling. And let the bandage be stretched over the dislocated part itself and over its lower edge."
"And then two posts should be placed, one under the foot and one under the head. And another post should be adjusted in the middle of the elbow with damp cloths wrapped around it so that the patient is not harmed by the post being between their bones, for fear that the extension towards the outer part might pull on it."
"And then each assistant should extend to his part, and the physician with his hand should guide the reduction according to the art."
"But when the dislocation is at the inner part, then let the bandage be extended lightly. And this is how it is done on the elbow: and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated collarbone and then presses that with his other hand while extending it towards the outer part towards the shoulder blade."
"But when the dislocation is at the back part, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended towards the outer part, but rather they should be elevated on the ground. And it must be placed on a hard surface, just as in the case of an ankle dislocation outward, according to what we have said about the elevation of it onto two ankles; and this is done with their face downwards and the bandages extended as we have said."
"And compression should be administered with a board over the place where the joint goes."
"So when the reduction is complete, you will know that it has been done correctly if when you extend both bandages and the patient pulls on them and extends their arm without difficulty, then you will know that the member has been returned properly. And then you should adjust two splints and apply pressure firmly while extending with a bandage so that the ankle is not moved from either part; and let the patient remain at rest for three or four days."
"And then untie the bandages and the pressure, and compare one splint with the other."
"Then if you find that those joints are firm, then you will know that the dislocation has been fixed, and at that point you should let the patient go."
"And if you find any sign of loosening, then return and splint it again, extend it according to your first traction, and let it remain for three days."
"And then release that, and let the patient walk about on each foot alternately for several days until they are strengthened."
Chapter XXXII
Of the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade can be dislocated in three ways: outward, inward, and more often toward the outer side than towards the back part. It is not dislocated towards the front part."
The sign of dislocation of it is that when you command the patient to apply their arm with the elbow, then you will know that the shoulder blade is dislocated."
And the restoration of all three kinds is that you make the patient sit upright so that they can extend their arm if possible; then press your two hands on its face and place the patient's foot between your feet. Then bring the shoulder blade back with both palms, holding it between your hands strongly until the other minister extends the patient's foot until the shoulder blade returns to its proper place."
"It is a sign of recovery that their bed adheres to them without labor."
"And then, apply this plaster and make the bed adhere to them. Also, tie those wounds with a bandage for three or four days, and then untie them."
"The patient should not use their legs for some days until they are healed and strengthened."
"However, if the reduction of this one is excused, you should then administer a strong extension with traction from those ligaments which were involved in the previous hip operation."
Chapter XXXIII.
About the treatment of ankle dislocation.
The ankle sometimes becomes displaced with slight motion and sometimes becomes dislocated upon completion; its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of ankle dislocation is that sometimes the ankle appears swollen at the site where it is dislocated.
"The cure for moving him is easy to accomplish: he should be gently extended with his hands and turned until he returns."
"However, in the case of a dislocation treated according to all the rules, it is necessary that you keep the patient on their right side, and one assistant must be strong and skilled at placing his hand behind the patient's back in the middle of it."
"And with your right hand grasp the upper part of the thigh near the hip, and with your left hand grasp the lower part of the thigh near the knee."
"And then pull the thigh towards you with your right hand, and hold it there with your left hand for two or three times, and then extend it with your left hand; and press the head of the bone against the hip socket without shaking."
"And perform this procedure twice as we have described."
"And then press the head of the bone into the hip socket on the third attempt, and you are at the ilium."
Therefore if this method is returned to once or twice again, and if the foot swells, that's good; otherwise, then repeat the operation, because that returns.
But if you are forbidden from reducing the hernia with what we have mentioned, then make the patient lie on their side on the ground, and insert a small stone between their thighs, secured firmly to prevent slipping. Then wrap a cloth around them so that they do not move.
And then the minister of the testicles should hold their scrotum, and another minister should extend the leg with its thigh or bandage that lies on the thigh; and each minister should stretch out their own side, and the stone should remain between the thighs of the sick person holding it, so that the body does not move towards its extension.
And then the doctor reduces the dislocation with their hands, and another minister holds the healthy thigh on the outside to prevent displacement.
And then when the dislocation returns and it is declared to you that the reduction has been successful, then apply plaster and secure it with bandages very tightly. Then tie the thigh with bandages at the knee.
Be careful or guard yourself from the nerves which are behind the heel, for if you bind it tightly there it will cause pain and hurt them greatly.
And then leave him like that for two or three days; and if the bandage becomes softened, remove it.
And then on the third day or the fourth day, remove the splint from him, and forbid the patient to move for forty days. But if he is forced to walk during that time, it is not guaranteed that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted again, and thus would not receive proper treatment.
But if an abscess forms from the wound, then you should apply the remedies that were mentioned earlier in this narrative regarding cure and embrocation until it is removed.
Chapter XXXIV
Of the treatment of dislocation of the said feet.
It is necessary that one who has suffered a dislocation should be reduced with little extension without struggle; and it should not be difficult to accomplish this, for the leg is lighter.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the toes of the foot, then you should have the patient lie on their back on the ground or on a table, and hold their leg extended as if they were walking.
And then you stand on your feet, and place your hand beneath the protruding joints, and press down with your foot with force until it returns into its proper position, and make sure that it is properly reduced and does not protrude again.
And then place the sole of the foot on a table that takes up all the leg, which has two ends. Then stretch it properly and firmly with bandages for three days. Then remove them and have him rest from walking for many days until he is healed and the reduction is secure.
Chapter XXXV
"Of dislocation injuries that occur with bruising or fracture or in combination."
When some cause of those occurs, and you perform the cure of that and the restoration of it, many times death succeeds.
"And therefore it is not necessary that one presume about its cure, but rather he who is wise in the art, experienced through long practice, merciful and compassionate, careful, not reckless or presumptuous; and let him begin with medicines that draw out only abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, but also to him whom you hope for healing from destruction, with alleviation of the illness, and in which some hope appears." And therefore strive immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before an abscess forms.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, afterwards administer regimen which draws out abscess and heals the bruising with whatever is convenient to it of the dried remedies.
And if dislocation occurs with fracture and the broken ends of the bone are displaced, then you perform the reduction of them, and do in this what we have said in the simple cases of that which is precedent in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that which enters through the paths of fear, as my prediction has preceded for you; because this is better for your Creator than you deserve, and more lasting for your glory, and more healthy for your true body in your time. Thus endeth the words of Albucasis perfectly, may God bless them! Amen.
